I never saw (which is strange), but I think that it's possible. </p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>In general, conservation laws are the most fundamental laws of physics, and all of classical mechanics can be derived from them. In particular, Newton's laws can be derived from conservation of energy and momentum.</p>\n\n<p>For the law of the lever, you can derive it from conservation of energy for the case where the forces at the ends are weights that have some potential energy. Once the law is found in this case, it can be applied to all other cases, since the equilibrium of the lever is independent of the types of forces applied to it.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://physics.stackexchange.com/questions/74092/rotary-steam-engine","title":"Rotary Steam Engine","creationDate":1376418454,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"physics","questionID":74092,"body":"<p>Leon Crux invented the concept of interlocking archimedes spirals that eventually led to the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scroll_compressor\" rel=\"nofollow\">scroll compressor</a> but intended it to be used as a rotary steam engine. Hence, the buoyancy force doesn't change with a denser fluid. Instead the displaced volume decreases to cancel out the effect of the increased fluid density.</p>\n\n<p>For an object that doesn't float, the displaced volume needed for it to float is larger than the voulme of the object.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://physics.stackexchange.com/questions/58138/archimedes-principle-and-specific-gravity","title":"Archimedes principle and specific gravity","creationDate":1364330653,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"physics","questionID":58138,"body":"<p>A physical balance measures the gravitational mass of a body. I conducted an experiment to find out the specific gravity of a bob. I first measured the mass of the bob in air, and then in water. The mass of the bob in water was less than that in the air. Mass remains constant. How can it change in water? If it was not mass that decreased but it was the weight of the bob, then why did we use physical balance to find out the specific gravity of bob? Mass is measured in grams, or kilograms and weight in Newtons, slug or pound, etc. What I noticed was that the quantity (Mass or weight) decreased from 28.75 gm to 25 gm. Can kilogram or gram and Newton be  used interchangeably?  </p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>The mass of the object always stays the same. The balance can only measure the downward force exerted on it by the bob. The force measured by the balance is simply the weight of the masses on one side needed to balance the downward force of the bob on the other side.</p>\n\n<p>In air, the only appreciable force will be the downward force from gravity, aka the weight of the bob. In water, there is also a significant upward force due to the buoyant force exerted on the bob by the water. So in the water, the balance is measuring the difference between the weight of the bob and the buoyant force. The relevant physics and formulas can all be found on Wikipedia easily. If you define the (true) specific gravity $S$ as the ratio of the density of your bob $\\rho_B$ to the density of water, i.e. $S = \\frac{\\rho_B}{\\rho_{H_20}}$, you should be able to show that\n$$ \\frac{f_{water}}{f_{air}} = 1 - \\frac{1}{S},$$\nwhere $f_{air}$ and $f_{water}$ are the forces measured by your balance in air and in water respectively.</p>\n\n<p>Regarding the use of grams or Newtons, they are often used interchangeably to talk about the <strong>weight</strong> of an object, although this is technically rather sloppy because they are <strong>not the same thing in general</strong>. The two units measure fundamentally different things, one is a mass and one is a force. However, since all objects on the Earth are subject to the same acceleration $g$ due to gravity, there is a natural way to change between one and the other, by the formula $f = mg$. Whenever people use grams to measure forces, or Newtons to measure mass, it is this correspondence that they are implicitly referring to.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://physics.stackexchange.com/questions/43051/dropping-an-anchor-from-a-boat","title":"Dropping an anchor from a boat","creationDate":1351610060,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"physics","questionID":43051,"body":"<blockquote>\n  <p>A yacht on a lake drops its anchor overboard. Once you put the anchor in the water the water level still rises but not as much so the overall water level drops slightly.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://physics.stackexchange.com/questions/33740/why-is-there-a-wave-on-the-water-surface-when-i-put-something-in-the-water","title":"Why is there a wave on the water surface when I put something in the water?","creationDate":1344449771,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"physics","questionID":33740,"body":"<p>We've started doing waves in our physics class, but we're doing things very quickly and the teacher doesn't explain anything. And I don't understand why waves work. </p>\n\n<p>I was thinking that when I fill a bowl with water, and wait until it's still, and then I put a finger in it, then my finger needs some space where the water is, so the water has to move away, like Archimedes said. So some of that water goes up because it's the only place where there's no finger or the rest of the water, and it's easier for the water to push away the air than the finger or the rest of the water. I think it's only the displacement thing that's working here because I tried putting the finger in the water faster, and the effect was pretty much the same except I spilled a lot of water trying different things. </p>\n\n<p>But I don't get it why after the water has formed this something like a wall around my finger (not that I can see this moment; it's too quick), the wall starts to move towards the rim. Why doesn't the water just flow down the wall, like dry sand flows down a sand wall? Then I think the excess water should spread itself over the surface, but it doesn't do that. So how does this work?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>When you first put your finger into the water, the situation is pretty much as you describe; the water is not solid and so the molecules <em>can</em> move, unlike those of your finger or the bowl, so they <em>must</em> move (no two atoms can be in exactly the same place at exactly the same time). Liquids are classically defined as incompressible (technically most liquids including water <em>can</em> be compressed, but the ratio of force to volume is <em>much</em> greater than with a gas), so not only must the water molecules move, they must push all the other water molecules out of the way instead of simply getting closer to them. However, air, a gas, <em>can</em> move and <em>can</em> compress, so initially it is easier for the water to move up against the air than out against more water to get out of the way of your finger.</p>\n\n<p>That's what happens in the first fractions of a second after your finger enters. What happens next involves some very complex math, but the concept is still simple; while water is incompressible, it is still a fluid, and so as gravity pulls on all the water molecules, those molecules move around each other trying to reach a new equilibrium between the force of gravity and the forces exerted by all the other molecules of water. In short, the surface of the water wants to settle down to a new \"level\". The water that moved up when you inserted your finger now tries to move back down, pulled by gravity. That pushes other water molecules aside, just as your finger did, and with a similar result; some of them find it easier to go up than out. This process repeats itself over time, with the result that a wave of water is generated as water molecules get out of the way of other molecules and then settle. When this wave hits the edges of the bowl, well, that's a solid surface that won't move, so the water can't move out, so it moves up again, and then when it settles there's only one way the water can push and that's back in the opposite direction.</p>\n\n<p>Now, the second law of thermodynamics states that \"you can't get the same energy out of a system that you put in\"; even at the molecular level, there is \"friction\", where molecules will simply absorb the energy as heat instead of moving. In addition, as waves bounce around the edges of the bowl, they meet, and when the crest of the wave meets a trough, the two cancel out (the sum of the energies of the peak and trough of a waveform of given amplitude is zero). Eventually, the waves cancel out enough that the water molecules themselves simply absorb the energy and do not move (perceptibly) at all, and the system has then reached its new resting state.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://physics.stackexchange.com/questions/4831/how-long-would-a-lever-have-to-be-to-move-the-planet-earth","title":"How long would a lever have to be to move the planet Earth?","creationDate":1297201671,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"physics","questionID":4831,"body":"<blockquote>\n  <p>Give me a lever long enough and a\n  fulcrum on which to place it, and I\n  shall move the world.\n                <br>-Archimedes</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>How long would that lever have to be?</p>\n\n<p>That is to say, how long a lever would be needed on Earth, to lift a sphere with the mass of Earth if a human of average size were to sit on the other side of the lever?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>The mass of Earth is $6\\times10^{24}kg$. If Archimedes can lift 60 kg, he would need a lever with an arm ratio of $10^{23}:1$. So if the short arm is one meter long, the lever length will be $10^{23}$ meters plus one. Also, note that he would have to push the lever for $10^{20}$ meters to shift the Earth just by one millimeter.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1390891/false-proof-pi-4-but-why","title":"False proof: $\\pi = 4$, but why?","creationDate":1439136482,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1390891,"body":"<p>Note: Over the course of this summer, I have taken both Geometry and Precalculus, and I am very excited to be taking Calculus 1 next year (Sophomore for me). In this question, I will use things that I know from Calculus, but I emphasize that <em>I have not taken the course,</em> so please bear with me. This will be long.</p>\n\n<p>Among other Geometric formulas that I have learned recently, I am currently trying to prove that the surface area of a sphere is $4\\pi r^2$. Intuitively, this seems fairly straightforward for me, since I have already proven the volume of a sphere using integration to be $\\frac{4}{3} \\pi r^3$, and that the integral of the circumference of a circle is its area. Using these two facts together, it makes sense that the integral of the surface area of a sphere should be its volume, leading me to believe the formula for surface area stated above.</p>\n\n<p>However, this is a very weak argument, since I make the connection between circumference to area and area to volume without much proof. As an alternative, I made a proof involving infinite sums of the lateral areas of cylinders squeezed into a sphere. One can use this method of infinite cylinders to prove the volume of a sphere, but when I tried almost the exact same thing for surface area, I found that <strong>the value of pi is 4</strong>.</p>\n\n<p>I have only heard of one other strange proof that leads to this result, and it can be found here: <a href=\"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/12906/is-value-of-pi-4\">Is value of $\\pi = 4$</a>. It was promptly disproved in numerous ways, some of them here:<a href=\"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/43118/how-to-convince-a-layman-that-the-pi-4-proof-is-wrong\">How to convince a layman that the π=4 proof is wrong?</a></p>\n\n<p>As far as I know, there's nothing wrong with my math -- I most likely simply set up the problem incorrectly. Here's what I did:</p>\n\n<p>Consider a sphere of radius $r$, aligned with the coordinate plane in a way that its center is the origin. To approximate the surface area (or volume) of this sphere, we can imagine fitting $n$ stacked cylinders into it, each with a center on the line $x = 0$. Each cylinder has a height $h$, or $\\Delta y$. \n<a href=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/xayEu.png\"><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/xayEu.png\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></a></p>\n\n<p>Since the height of the sphere is $2r$, </p>\n\n<p>$h = \\frac{2r}{n}$</p>\n\n<p>Looking at a cross-section of this sphere, the radius of each cylinder must satisfy the equation:</p>\n\n<p>$r_i^2 + y^2 = r^2$</p>\n\n<p>where $r_i$ is the radius of the cylinder with index $i$, and the radius of the sphere is $r$. Now, we sum the lateral areas of the cylinders to get an approximation for the surface area of the sphere. As $n \\rightarrow \\infty$, our approximation gets better, so</p>\n\n<p>$A = \\lim_{n\\rightarrow\\infty}{\\sum_{i=1}^n{2\\pi r_i (\\frac{2r}{n})}} = \\lim_{n\\rightarrow\\infty}{\\sum_{i=1}^n{2\\pi r_i \\Delta y}}$</p>\n\n<p>In other words:</p>\n\n<p>$\\int_{-r}^r{2\\pi r_i   dy}$</p>\n\n<p>And as we already know,</p>\n\n<p>$r_i = \\sqrt{r^2-y_i^2}$</p>\n\n<p>So we substitute and take out constants:</p>\n\n<p>$2\\pi \\int_{-r}^r{\\sqrt{r^2-y^2} dy}$</p>\n\n<p>From here, we have two options: 1) Take the integral and find something very messy; 2) Recognize that the expression inside the integral is just a semicircle, and that a semicircle has half the area of a circle.</p>\n\n<p>Choosing option 2), I end up with: </p>\n\n<p>$2\\pi \\frac{\\pi r^2}{2} = \\pi^2 r^2$</p>\n\n<p>Which is clearly not the surface area of a sphere, but I can't figure out why. Strangely, another \"proof\" I did also led to this result. Take the semicircle $y = \\sqrt{r^2 - x^2}$ with arclength $\\pi r$ and rotate it about the x axis $2\\pi$ radians. We now have a sphere, with surface area $2\\pi^2 r^2$. Something is clearly wrong here, but it gets stranger. If we simultaneously accept Archimedes proof of the surface area of a sphere, we find:</p>\n\n<p>$\\pi^2 r^2 = 4\\pi r^2$</p>\n\n<p>And by \"solving for $\\pi$,\" we find that: $\\pi = 4$.</p>\n\n<p>I'm not looking for a better proof or someone to convince me that Archimedes is right, as I fully accept the textbook formula and have used other proofs to show it. I have a feeling that I may have approached the sphere in a \"non-smooth\" way, since the zig-zag shape that the cylinders make is eerily like the method used in the classic $\\pi = 4$ proof, and when I used polygonal approximations I got a valid answer. Thanks for reading all the way through this, and does anyone know how I messed up?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>One thing that's true about volume but not surface area (or, down a dimension, true about area but not perimeter) is that if shape $A$ contains shape $B$, then $A$ has a larger volume than $B$. In symbols:\n$$A\\supseteq B\\implies\\operatorname{volume}(A)\\ge\\operatorname{volume}(B)$$\nThis means that it's fairly easy to approximate volumes — just find the volume of something that contains $A$ to get an upper bound, and find the volume of something that's contained in $A$ to get a lower bound. Now, to get the volume exactly: if we can get better and better approximations, then we can just take the limit as the two approximations get increasingly better. Hopefully, they approach the same limit (call it $a$) — and, except for weird cases, they usually do approach the same limit — so we have that the volume of $A$ is bounded above <em>and</em> below by $a$. That is, $a\\le\\operatorname{volume}(A)\\le a$. Thus, we have that the volume is exactly $a$.</p>\n\n<p>This all goes out the window with surface area. Let's say $A$ is a smooth shape, and $B$ is some really spiky thing contained in $A$. Now, it's entirely possible that $B$ has a larger surface area than $A$. So we get no upper or lower bounds. This means that we have no reason to expect our approximations to be accurate whatsoever. And, as you've found out, they aren't always accurate.</p>\n\n<p>There is a way to save this. If $A$ and $B$ are both convex, then we <em>do</em> have that $A$ has a larger surface area than $B$, and our approximations <em>do</em> work. And we <em>can</em> use limits to get the volume exactly. However, I'm pretty sure your union of cylinders isn't convex.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1387028/space-capsule-volume-of-revolution","title":"Space capsule volume of revolution","creationDate":1438888323,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1387028,"body":"<p>We are contracting you to help design a space capsule for NASA that will orbit the earth and eventually re-enter the atmosphere and splash down in the Indian Ocean. (Archimedes area of parabola)","creationDate":1438367753,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1380517,"body":"<p>I am currently reading Apostol's Calculus volume 1 and was revising the part where the area of a parabolic segment is found. I decided to write my own proof similar to the one in the book, which I found too complicated to really understand (not logically, but just motivation wise because he doesn't explain things like how you can think of $1^2+2^2+\\cdots+(n-1)^2 &lt; n^3/3 &lt; 1^2+2^2+\\cdots+n^2$ and it just seems like he pulled a rabbit out of the hat when he states this inequality).</p>\n\n<p>My main question is whether my proof is rigorous. Here goes:</p>\n\n<p>We want to find the area of the parabola that has an ordinate $ax^2+c$ at $x$. We want to find this area from $x=0$ to $x=b$. So first we find upper and lower bounds, $\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}f(n-1)+\\frac{(n-1)bc}{n}&lt;A&lt;\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}f(n)+bc$ where $A$ is the area. This must be true for all $n\\in \\mathbb{Z}^+$. Our plan is simple: We try to \"build\" this inequality from a \"primitive\" inequality for which we know the middle term. In this way, if we can make the middle term constant at some step in the manipulation, and keep it constant, then by comparison we will know $A$. This is because, we will have an inequality of the form $\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}f(n-1)+\\frac{(n-1)bc}{n}&lt;C&lt;\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}f(n)+bc$ for some constant $C$, and this will be true for all $n$. Therefore, since the area of a parabolic segment cannot have two values from $x=0$ to $x=b$, it must be that $A=C$, and we will know $A$ since we know $C$.</p>\n\n<p>Now, what can this \"primitive inequality\" that we talked about look like. Well, it must be true for all $n\\in \\mathbb{Z}^+$, so that when we manipulate it into the desired form we end up with the \"$C$\" inequality being true for all $n$. Therefore it must be an inequality in $n$.</p>\n\n<p>Also we see $f(n-1)$ and $f(n)$. Eventually therefore we might be led to consider the primitive inequality $f(n-1)&lt;g(n)&lt;f(n)$ for some function $g(n)$. We know that we must multiply this through by $\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}$. We also know that we want $g(n)$ to be constant after this multiplication. Thus we consider $g(n)=kn^3$ for some constant $k$. It is easy to find therefore that $k=1/3$; indeed $f(n-1)&lt;n^3/3&lt;f(n)$ is true for all $n\\in \\mathbb{Z}^+$. Now we start:</p>\n\n<p>Multplying through by $\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}$ gives $\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}f(n-1)&lt;\\frac{ab^3}{3}&lt;\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}f(n)$ (the middle term constant like we wanted), then we add $bc$ to both sides rather than $\\frac{(n-1)bc}{n}$ (to keep the middle term constant) to get $\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}f(n-1)+bc&lt;\\frac{ab^3}{3}+bc&lt;\\frac{ab^3}{n^3}f(n)+bc$. So by comparison with the original inequality, we see that $A=\\frac{ab^3}{3}+bc$ and we are done.</p>\n","isAnswered":false},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1371668/equation-to-place-points-equidistantly-on-an-archimedian-spiral-using-arc-length","title":"Equation to place It's part of his retelling of Archimedes method for finding the integral of $x^2$. </p>\n\n<p>At one point, the conclusion is reached</p>\n\n<p><em>Therefore any number $A$ must also satisfy</em>\n$$\\frac{b^3}{3} - \\frac{b^3}{n} &lt; A &lt; \\frac{b^3}{3} + \\frac{b^3}{n}$$ \n<em>for every integer $n \\geq 1$. Now there are only thee possibilities:</em>\n$$A &gt; \\frac{b^3}{3}, A &lt; \\frac{b^3}{3}, A = \\frac{b^3}{3}$$\nAfterwards Apostle goes on to prove that the first two possibilities lead to a contradiction and that the third option has to be correct. But this is where I'm confused. Why must it hold for all $n$ in the third case if it doesn't hold for all $n$ in the first two? It's not exactly the same as when you have 3 excluding cases. We're assuming that because it only holds for some $n$ in the first two cases that it therefore must hold for all $n$ in the third. Where am I going wrong here? </p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Note that \"for all integers $n \\geq 1$'' goes with the first sentence of the quote, not the second.  The alternatives in the second sentence do not depend on $n$, but rather compare two constants.</p>\n\n<p>In his version of Archimedes' proof, Apostol assumes $A &gt; \\frac{b^3}{3}$ and then uses the first statement of the quote -- that for all integers $n \\geq 1$, $A$ is between $\\frac{b^3}{3}-\\frac{b^3}{n}$ and $\\frac{b^3}{3}+\\frac{b^3}{n}$ -- to show that all positive integers $n$ must be less than a finite constant $\\frac{b^3}{A-\\frac{b^3}{3}}$.  There are infinitely many $n$ greater than that constant, so we have infinitely many contradictions and the original assumption must be false.  The original assumption $A &gt; \\frac{b^3}{3}$ doesn't depend on $n$.  The second alternative $A &lt; \\frac{b^3}{3}$ can be disposed of with a similar argument.  Thus, since $A$ isn't greater than $\\frac{b^3}{3}$ and $A$ isn't less than $\\frac{b^3}{3}$, $A$ must be equal to $\\frac{b^3}{3}$.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1280712/can-we-still-learn-from-the-old-masters","title":"Can we still learn from the old masters?","creationDate":1431541272,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1280712,"body":"<p>So, let me first describe how my doubt originated: out of curiosity I started to study Newton's Opticks, a book written more than 300 years ago. \\tan\\big(\\frac{\\pi}n\\big)=n \\Big(\\frac{\\pi }{n}+\\frac{\\pi ^3}{3 n^3}+\\cdots)=\\pi+\\frac{\\pi ^3}{3 n^2}+\\cdots$$ For the second (without using the good hint user222031 provided in \\cos\\big(\\frac{2\\pi}n\\big)=\\frac{\\pi ^2}{n^2}-\\frac{\\pi ^4}{3 n^4}+\\cdots=\\frac{\\pi ^2}{n^2}\\big(1-\\frac{\\pi ^2}{3n^2}+\\cdots\\big)$$ $$\\sqrt{\\frac 12 -\\frac 12 \\cos\\big(\\frac{2\\pi}n\\big)}=\\pi\\big(1-\\frac{\\pi ^2}{6n^2}+\\cdots\\big)$$</p>\n\n<p>For sure, this be can be done faster using  user222031's hint since  $$\\sqrt{\\frac 12 -\\frac 12 ^2}{6n^2}+\\cdots\\big)$$</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1260257/if-the-greeks-had-been-four-dimensional-would-they-have-been-able-to-derive-the","title":"If the Greeks had been four dimensional, would they have been able to derive the pi squared coefficient for the hypersphere volume without calculus?","creationDate":1430461229,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1260257,"body":"<p>I was reading about Archimedes' pre-calculus proof of the volume of the sphere and I realized that the trick he uses (volume of hemisphere + volume of cone = volume of cylinder) doesn't generalize to hyperspheres, so I was wondering if there's any way of proving the volume of a hypersphere is $\\frac{π^2}{2}R^4$ with classical methods.</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Your solution posted in the given link is so nice that it deserves to come alive here.</p>\n\n<p>Consider a four-dimensional ball of radius $R$:\n$$B:=\\bigl\\{(x,y,u,v)\\&gt;\\bigm|\\&gt;x^2+y^2+u^2+v^2\\leq R^2\\bigr\\}\\ ,$$\nand at the same time a four-dimensional dicone\n$$C:=\\bigl\\{(x,y,u,v)\\&gt;\\bigm|\\&gt;u^2+v^2\\leq x^2+y^2\\leq R^2\\bigr\\}\\ .$$\nDue to symmetry the  dicone $C$  is just half of the dicylinder\n$$Z:=\\bigl\\{(x,y,u,v)\\&gt;\\bigm|\\&gt;u^2+v^2\\leq R^2, \\ x^2+y^2\\leq R^2\\bigr\\}\\ ,$$\nso that\n$${\\rm vol}_4(C)={1\\over2}{\\rm vol}_4(Z)\\ .\\tag{1}$$\nNow comes Archimedes.  The following figure shows the projections of the three bodies onto the $(x,y)$-plane placed next to each other:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/EPDQd.jpg\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></p>\n\n<p>If we erect a two-dimensional ''stalk'' at $(x,y)\\in B'$ this stalk will intersect $B$ in the $(u,v)$-disk\n$$\\bigl\\{(u,v)\\&gt;\\bigm|\\&gt;u^2+v^2\\leq R^2-x^2-y^2\\bigr\\}$$\nof area $\\pi(R^2-x^2-y^2)$, and the stalk erected at $(x,y)\\in C'$  will intersect $C$ in the $(u,v)$-disk\n$$\\bigl\\{(u,v)\\&gt;\\bigm|\\&gt;u^2+v^2\\leq x^2+y^2\\bigr\\}$$\nof area $\\pi(x^2+y^2)$. The sum of these two areas is $=\\pi R^2$ for all $(x,y)$, and is equal to the area that such a stalk cuts out of $Z$. By Cavalieri's principle we therefore can conclude that\n$${\\rm vol}_4(B)+{\\rm vol}_4(C)={\\rm vol}_4(Z)\\ .$$\nTogether with $(1)$ it follows that\n$${\\rm vol}_4(B)={1\\over2}{\\rm vol}_4(Z)={\\pi^2\\over2}R^4\\ .$$</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1192054/help-on-apostols-explanation-of-archimedes-method","title":"Help on Apostol&#39;s explanation of Archimedes method?","creationDate":1426491753,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1192054,"body":"<p>I'm reading Apostol's <em>Calculus</em>, I don't understand what he's doing here (I've searched a bit, found explanations on <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_pyramidal_number\" rel=\"nofollow\">Square Pyramidal Numbers</a>, Faulhaber's formula and Ehrhart polynomials) but none of them seem to be similar to the explanation Apostol gives here:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/UrQ2I.jpg\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></p>\n  \n  <p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/jiFjy.jpg\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I guess I just need a little bit more of context. </p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Note that $(k+1)^3-k^3$ has no term with power $3$. Thus, he you can write the differences $(k+1)^3-k^3$ only on terms of $k^2, k$, which is precisely what you want; since the sum\n$$\n\\sum_{k=1}^{n-1} (k+1)^3-k^3\n$$\nis telescopic, it equals $n^3-1$. On the other hand, you have\n$$n^3-1=\\sum_{k=1}^{n-1} (k+1)^3-k^3=\\sum_{k=1}^{n-1} 3k^2+3k+1,$$\nso you can now isolate $n^3$ to get your desired expression. Summarizing, the trick of considering $(k+1)^3-k^3=3k^2+3k+1$ is to get rid of the $k^3$ term and moreover to obtain a telescopic sum whenever you sum up those expressions. Eventually you could check if you got the trick by computing $\\sum_{k=1}^n k^4$.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1173645/why-does-archimedes-method-to-calculate-pi-decrease-in-precision-after-a-certain","title":"Why does Archimedes Method to calculate Pi decrease in precision after a certain time?","creationDate":1425397445,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1173645,"body":"<p>i`m using the following recursive formula to calculate Pi based on Archimedes ideas.</p>\n\n<p>$$\nS' = \\sqrt{2-\\sqrt{4-S^2}}\n$$\nThe formula gives back the edge length of a Polygon B based on the edge length of the Polygon A. Try changing these, and see what effect it has on the result.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1155170/line-integral-over-archimedess-spiral","title":"Line integral over Archimedes&#39;s spiral","creationDate":1424297531,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1155170,"body":"<p>Compute $\\int \\limits_{C} F.dr$ for $F(x,y)=(x,y)$ and $C$ is the Archimedes's spiral given in polar coordinates by $r=1+\\theta$ for $\\theta \\in [0,2\\pi]$</p>\n\n<p>How do I parametrize this? And how do find I the limits?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Switching the vector field $\\vec{F}$ to polar coordinates, we get $\\vec{F}(r,\\theta) = (r\\cos\\theta, r \\sin\\theta)$. Now, since we are on Archimedes's spiral, we can substitute $r = 1 + \\theta$ to find \n$$\n\\vec{F}(\\theta) = \\bigg( (1+\\theta)\\cos\\theta, (1+\\theta)\\sin\\theta \\bigg)\n$$\nSimilarly, \n\\begin{align*}\nd\\vec{r} = \\bigg( dx, dy \\bigg) &amp;= \\bigg( \\cos\\theta dr -r\\sin\\theta d\\theta, \\sin\\theta dr + r\\cos\\theta d\\theta \\bigg)\\\\\n&amp;= \\bigg( \\cos\\theta d\\theta -(1+\\theta)\\sin\\theta d\\theta, \\sin\\theta d\\theta +(1+\\theta)\\cos\\theta d\\theta \\bigg)\\\\\n&amp;= \\bigg( \\left( \\cos\\theta -(1+\\theta)\\sin\\theta \\right)d\\theta, \\left(\\sin\\theta+(1+\\theta)\\cos\\theta \\right)d\\theta \\bigg)\n\\end{align*}\nAs we are only interested in the portion of the spiral cut out by the angles $\\theta \\in [0,2\\pi]$, the line integral becomes\n\\begin{align*}\n\\int_C \\vec{F} \\cdot d\\vec{r} \n&amp;= \\int_{0}^{2\\pi} (1+\\theta)\\cos^2\\theta -(1+\\theta)^2 \\cos\\theta\\sin\\theta  + (1+\\theta)\\sin^2\\theta + (1+\\theta)^2 \\sin\\theta\\cos\\theta d\\theta\\\\\n&amp;= \\int_0^{2\\pi} (1+\\theta)d\\theta\\\\\n&amp;=\\theta + \\frac{\\theta^2}{2} \\bigg|_0^{2\\pi}\\\\\n&amp;= 2\\pi + 2\\pi^2.\n\\end{align*}\nIt's important in this problem to distinguish between the two terms $r$ and $\\vec{r}$, which play very different roles: $r$ is the variable for the polar coordinates in the plane, and $\\vec{r}$ is the radial vector in the line integral.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1154944/squaring-of-the-circle","title":"Squaring of the circle","creationDate":1424285728,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1154944,"body":"<p>From curiosity I read about enhancements of the squaring of the circle. A particle is discovered if we can measure its existence in nature. A law is discovered if the predictions it makes are observed in nature.</p>\n\n<p>Is this the case with mathematics? Is mathematics \"invented\", in the sense that we think up concepts and ways to relate them and all mathematical derivations are just toying with those base elements we made up, or \"discovered\", in the sense that there is some underlying fundamental \"natural\" framework we shed light on?</p>\n\n<p>On one hand, one could think that mathematics is an invention, as it usually derived from a set of axioms and all we do is take those axioms as given and creatively work from there, but on the other hand, there seem to be truths akin to physical laws or constants. Take $\\pi$ for example. Mathematicians in India computed the same number as Archimedes. If mathematics was just an invention by man, how could there be an agreement on this as there is with any other fundamental physical law? Same with the binomial theorem, for example. This suggests that mathematical truths are \"discovered\", not merely \"invented\".</p>\n\n<p>In fact, does it even make sense to ask this? Is this an open philosophical question?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>I would say, personally, that axioms are invented, and theorems are discovered (as a result of those axioms). Different people will probably have different opinions.</p>\n\n<p>An analogy may be that by inventing axioms, we are planting the seed of a tree. The resulting tree that grows from that seed is the mathematical framework that is a result of our planting of the tree, but we didn't have a part in shaping each branch.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1091225/continuous-functions-and-existence-of-a-root","title":"Continuous functions and existence of a root","creationDate":1420409507,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1091225,"body":"<p>Let $\\, f:[1,2] \\rightarrow \\mathbb R$ be a continuous function such that for every $n$ $\\in$ $\\mathbb N, \\exists$ $x \\in [1,2]$ with $\\ |f(x)| &lt; \\frac 1n$</p>\n\n<p>Show that $ \\exists \\;c \\in [1,2]$ such that $ \\;f(c)=0$</p>\n\n<p>So with this, I went by contradiction and said that (I'm being informal here) that if f(x) doesn't equal to 0 then the function completely lies on one side of the x axis then argued by Archimedes Principle saying there's a number with 1/n &lt; c (where c is the absolute min of the function in [1,2], which exists by Boundedness Property) leading to a contradiction.</p>\n\n<p>Is this an acceptable way to go about this proof?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Let $x_n\\in[1,2]$ such that $|f(x_n)|&lt;1/n$. Then, by the squeezing theorem,\n$$\n\\lim_{n\\to\\infty}f(x_n)=0\n$$\nbecause\n$$\n-\\frac{1}{n}&lt;f(x_n)&lt;\\frac{1}{n}.\n$$\nSince $[1,2]$ is compact, the sequence $(x_n)$ has a convergent subsequence $(x_{n_k})$ and if $x=\\lim_{k\\to\\infty}x_{n_k}$, then\n$$\nf(x)=\\lim_{k\\to\\infty}f(x_{n_k})=0\n$$\nby continuity.</p>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p>Your idea is good as well. If $f(x)\\ne0$ for all $x\\in[1,2]$, then we can assume $f(x)&gt;0$ (or consider $-f$, otherwise). Thus the minimum $f(c)$ of $f$ in $[1,2]$ is positive. segments are congruent in the arbelos","creationDate":1417157497,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1042014,"body":"<h2>Background Info + Problem</h2>\n\n<p>I teach HS Geometry to middle school age students. I generally like to try to solve problems instead of looking up the answer, but this week a student emailed me a problem that I don't think I'll be able to solve on my own. So I thought I'd ask it here since I haven't been able to find a solution online. <strong>The problem is to prove that $\\overline{AC} \\cong \\overline{EF}$.</strong> The diagram is known as the arbelos. Information can be found at <a href=\"http://mathworld.wolfram.com/Arbelos.html\" rel=\"nofollow\">wolfram|alpha</a>, <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arbelos\" rel=\"nofollow\">wikipedia</a>. The green circle in the construction is often referred to as one of the two \"twin-circles\" or \"Archimedes circles.\"</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/MkyPs.jpg\" alt=\"Basic problem diagram: prove AC\"></p>\n\n<h2>Here's a hint</h2>\n\n<p>I have researched this, and found the problem presented in a few places. I think that a distorted version of this problem was the inspiration for a study of the arbelos by H.P. Boas. Who, in the end of <a href=\"http://www.math.tamu.edu/~harold.boas/preprints/arbelos.pdf\" rel=\"nofollow\">his work (pdf)</a> poses the problem as an exercise to the reader. He offers the hint that </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>It suffices to show that $\\overline{AF} \\perp \\overline{EF}$, for then $\\triangle AFG \\sim \\triangle ECG$. Invert in a circle centered at A that cuts the right-hand twin circle orthogonally, and prove that the point F is left fixed.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>One sub-question to the main point, is a more general geometric question: Is there a good way to break this proof down into simpler parts? I could keep track of the various ratios throughout the 20+ steps of this construction, but that would be difficult to write and probably more difficult for someone else to follow.</p>\n\n<h2>Construction</h2>\n\n<p>If it is helpful, I will present the steps of the construction.</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li>Given $\\overline{AB}$ with midpoint $C$</li>\n<li>Construct $\\odot C$ with radius $\\overline{CA}$</li>\n<li>Let point $D$ lie on $\\overline{AB}$</li>\n<li>Points $E$ and $F$ are the midpoints of segments $AD$ and $DB$</li>\n<li>Construct circles $E$ with radius $\\overline{EA}$ and $F$ with radius $\\overline{FA}$. This is the basic arbelos.</li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>To construct the tangent or \"twin-circles\"</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li>Construct perpendicular lines to segment $AB$ through $E$ and $F$. Let point $G$ be the intersection point of the perpendicular and circle $E$ and point $H$ be the intersection point of the perpendicular and circle $F$.</li>\n<li>Let $I$ be the intersection of $FG$ and $EH$</li>\n</ol>\n\n<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/kC4ZB.png\" alt=\"Arbelos construction - ref 1\"></p>\n\n<ol start=\"3\">\n<li>Construct circle $D$ with radius $\\overline{DI}$</li>\n<li>Let J and K be the points where circle $D$ intersects segment $AB$</li>\n<li>Construct perpendiculars to segment $AB$ through $J$ and $K$</li>\n<li>At the intersection of the perpendicular from K and the circle with center F and radius $\\overline{FJ}$, construct circle $L$ with radius equal to $DK$</li>\n<li>At the intersection of $\\overrightarrow{FL}$ and the circle of center $F$ and radius $\\overline{FD}$ find point $M$, at the intersection of $\\overrightarrow{FL}$ and the perpendicular from D, find point $N$</li>\n</ol>\n\n<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/rhO3I.png\" alt=\"Update (#2) final construction\"></p>\n\n<p>In the construction I outlined above, I want to prove that $\\overline{AD} \\cong \\overline{MN}$.</p>\n\n<h1>How would you prove $\\overline{AD} \\cong \\overline{MN}$?</h1>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Restating my comment as an answer with diagrams (and slightly-different labeling) ...</p>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/4PuRg.png\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></p>\n\n<p>Let $X$ be the point of tangency of the twin circle $\\bigcirc R$ with $\\overline{CE}$, and $Y$ the point of tangency of $\\bigcirc R$ with the big semicircle, $\\stackrel{\\frown}{AB}$.</p>\n\n<p>Introduce $\\overline{AY}$ and $\\overline{BY}$, which form a right angle at $Y$. Convince yourself that $X$ is on $\\overline{AY}$, and that $E$ is on $\\overline{BY}$-extended. (This isn't <em>difficult</em>, but it's a bit tedious. See the \"footnote\" below.) Then $\\triangle ACX \\sim \\triangle ECB$.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/K74vE.png\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></p>\n\n<p>Now, let $Z$ be the midpoint of $\\overline{CX}$, which is in fact the center of $\\bigcirc CFX$. (Why?) Then $\\overline{AZ}$ and $\\overline{EQ}$ are corresponding medians of similar triangles, creating similar sub-triangles: $\\triangle ACZ \\sim \\triangle ECQ$. Consequently, $\\angle AXC$ is congruent to $\\angle EQC$, which is itself congruent to $\\angle FZX$ (why?); this implies that $A$, $Z$, $F$ are collinear. But $Z$ is such that $\\overline{ZF}\\perp\\overline{EF}$ (why?), and we conclude that $\\overline{AF}\\perp\\overline{EF}$.</p>\n\n<p>This is the result from Boas' hint (arrived-at via different means); from here, showing $\\overline{AC}\\cong\\overline{EF}$ is straightforward: $\\triangle ACZ$ and $\\triangle EFZ$ are similar right triangles with $\\overline{CZ} \\cong \\overline{FZ}$; therefore, the triangles are actually congruent, and $\\overline{AC}$ and $\\overline{EF}$ are corresponding parts. </p>\n\n<p><strong>Note.</strong> For some reason, I'm just not seeing Boas' suggestion to use an inversion to establish $\\overline{AF}\\perp\\overline{EF}$. I don't doubt that his approach is more elegant, especially since the problem is taken from an article celebrating the convenience of inversion. Even without an inversion, I think my argument could use considerable refinement. (It seems like we should be able to take better advantage of $\\bigcirc CFX$, which also passes through the intersection of $\\bigcirc P$ and $\\overline{AY}$.)</p>\n\n<p><strong>Note 2.</strong> It's worth mentioning that the radius of the twin circle is given by $\\frac{pq}{p+q}$, which is half the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic_mean\" rel=\"nofollow\">harmonic mean</a> of $p$ and $q$, where $p$ and $q$ are the radii of $\\bigcirc P$ and $\\bigcirc Q$. (Indeed, the construction @Nick used incorporates the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic_mean#In_geometry\" rel=\"nofollow\">\"crossed ladders\" derivation</a> of the mean.) The fact that this value is symmetric in $p$ and $q$ explains the \"twin\" part of the name \"twin circle\": the counterpart of $\\bigcirc R$ that is tangent to $\\bigcirc P$ has the same size.</p>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p><strong>Footnote.</strong> To show that $X$ is on $\\overline{AY}$ and $E$ is on $\\overline{BY}$-extended, consider the following, where I've introduced $O$ as the center of the large semicircle:</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/qGfkn.png\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></p>\n\n<p>Certainly, $\\overline{OY}$ passes through $R$, because $\\bigcirc O$ and $\\bigcirc R$ are tangent at $Y$. And, since $\\overline{RX}\\parallel\\overline{OA}$, we have that $\\triangle OAY$ and $\\triangle RXY$ are isosceles triangles with congruent vertex angles; they're similar, so that $\\angle OYA\\cong\\angle OYX$. This (along with the fact that $A$ and $X$ are on the same side of $\\overline{OY}$) implies that $A$, $X$, $Y$ are collinear.</p>\n\n<p>Likewise, $B$, $W$, $Y$ are collinear, where $W$ is the diametric opposite of $X$ in $\\bigcirc R$. Observe that $E$ lies on a line through $C$ and $X$, and on line through $R$ and $Q$ (not shown above, but shown earlier). $E$ is therefore the center of a dilation between $\\bigcirc R$ to $\\bigcirc Q$. Necessarily, $E$ is on the line through $W$ and $B$. And, since $\\angle QBW \\cong \\angle RWY$, it follows that $Y$ is collinear with $W$ and $B$ (and $E$). </p>\n\n<p>Of course, going through these details greatly amplifies the imperfection of this approach. I trust that there's a way to streamline my argument to avoid all these collinearity arguments, but I haven't found it.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1030284/show-f-is-integrable-and-integral-is-cb-a","title":"Show f is integrable and integral is C(b-a)","creationDate":1416456884,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1030284,"body":"<p>Let $f:[a,b]\\to\\Bbb R$ be as follows: $f(a)=A; f(b)=B$ and $f(x)=C$ for $a&lt;x&lt;b$. Show $f$ is integrable and the integral is $ C(b-a)$\nConsider for real $a&lt;b$ and real $A,B,C$, the function $f:[a,b] \\to \\mathbb R$ defined by</p>\n\n<p>$$f(x) = \\begin{cases} A &amp; x = a \\\\ B &amp; x=b \\\\ C &amp; a &lt; x &lt; b \\end{cases}$$</p>\n\n<p>I want to prove that this function is integrable and has integral $$\\int_a^b f = C(b-a)$$</p>\n\n<p>The hint I was given was to prove this by the Archimedes Riemann Theorem, which states that if $f:[a,b] \\to \\mathbb R$ is a bounded function, then $f$ is integrable if and only if there is a sequence $\\{P_n\\}$ of partitions of $[a,b]$ s.t. the limit $\\lim_{n \\to \\infty} [U(f,P_n)-L(f,P_n)]=0$, with $U$ and $L$ denoting the Lower and Upper Darboux sums, respectively.</p>\n\n<p>I know a previous post mentioned a hint\n<a href=\"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1026441/let-fa-b-to-bbb-r-be-as-follows-fa-a-fb-b-and-fx-c-for-axb/1026450?noredirect=1#comment2096350_1026450\">Let $f:[a,b]\\to\\Bbb R$ be as follows: $f(a)=A; f(b)=B$ and $f(x)=C$ for $a&lt;x&lt;b$. Show $f$ is integrable and the integral is $ C(b-a)$","creationDate":1416251439,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1026441,"body":"<p>Consider for real $a&lt;b$ and real $A,B,C$, the function $f:[a,b] \\to \\mathbb R$ defined by</p>\n\n<p>$$f(x) = \\begin{cases} A &amp; x = a \\\\ B &amp; x=b \\\\ C &amp; a &lt; x &lt; b \\end{cases}$$</p>\n\n<p>I want to prove that this function is integrable and has integral $$\\int_a^b f = C(b-a)$$</p>\n\n<p>The hint I was given was to prove this by the Archimedes Riemann Theorem, which states that if $f:[a,b] \\to \\mathbb R$ is a bounded function, then $f$ is integrable if and only if there is a sequence $\\{P_n\\}$ of partitions of $[a,b]$ s.t. the limit $\\lim_{n \\to \\infty} [U(f,P_n)-L(f,P_n)]=0$, with $U$ and $L$ denoting the Lower and Upper Darboux sums, respectively.</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p><strong>Hint:</strong> consider any sequence of partitions, such as\n$$\n\\left\\{a + k\\frac{b-a}n  \\right\\}_{0\\le k\\le n}\n$$</p>\n\n<p>An explicit computation proves that\n$$\nU(n,f) = (n-2)\\frac{b-a}n + \\frac {b-a}n (\\sup (A,C) + \\sup (C,B))\\\\\nL(n,f) = (n-2)\\frac{b-a}n + \\frac {b-a}n (\\inf (A,C) + \\inf (C,B))\\\\\n$$</p>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p>On the interval $[a,a + \\frac{b-a}n], f(x) = A$ or $C$, and so the bounds for the integral on this interval are\n$$\n\\left(  a + \\frac{b-a}n - a \\right) \\min(A,C) =  \\frac{b-a}n \\min(A,C) , \n\\frac{b-a}n \\max(A,C)\n$$</p>\n\n<p>On the intervals $[a + k \\frac{b-a}n,a + (k+1)\\frac{b-a}n]$ with $0&lt;k&lt;n-1$\n$f$ has the constant value $C$. Here both bounds are\n$$\n\\left(  a + \\frac{b-a}n - a \\right) C\n$$</p>\n\n<p>Eventually, on the interval $[a + (n-1) \\frac{b-a}n, b]$ \n$f(x) = B$ or $C$, and so the bounds for the integral on this interval are</p>\n\n<p>$$\n \\frac{b-a}n \\min(B,C) , \n\\frac{b-a}n \\max(B,C)\n$$</p>\n\n<p>When you make the sum of these bounds, you get the upper bound\n$$\nU(n,f) = (n-2)\\frac{b-a}n + \\frac {b-a}n (\\sup (A,C) + \\sup (C,B))\n$$</p>\n\n<p>and the lower bound\n$$\nL(n,f) = (n-2)\\frac{b-a}n + \\frac {b-a}n (\\inf (A,C) + \\inf (C,B))\n$$</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1006072/updated-why-didnt-archimedes-further-approximate-pi-this-way-or-did-he","title":"(UPDATED) Why didn&#39;t Archimedes further approximate $\\pi$ this way (or did he)?","creationDate":1415118943,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1006072,"body":"<p>Update is at bottom of my post.</p>\n\n<p>I saw on YouTube (<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_rJdkhlWZVQ\" rel=\"nofollow\">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_rJdkhlWZVQ</a>) a way to approximate $\\pi$ starting with a hexagon inscribed inside a circle of unit radius.  It uses formulas and relies on doubling the numbers of sides of the inscribed polygon (from $6$ to $12$ to $24$ to $48$ to $96$). To get the approximation of $\\pi$, I simply sum up (accumulate) the $3$rd entries from each tuple.  Notice how much quicker this is converging on the real value of $\\pi$ and with minimal work.</p>\n\n<p>Just playing around in my Excel spreadsheet with these values, I noticed that these approximations of $\\pi$ converge on the real value of $\\pi$ in a predictable way so I encoded this in the spreadsheet and I came up with an extrapolated estimate of $\\pi$ to be $3.14159265356529$ which I am quite happy with because it is as accurate as using about a $400,000$ sided polygon using Archimedes method but my method is much simpler.</p>\n\n<p>In a nutshell, I looked at the difference between the estimate from $12$ to $6$ and from $24$ to $12$... and I see that it is approximately \"quartering\" each time.  I took that a step farther and analyzed how close to $1/4$ the difference is at each doubling and encoded that as well and just from those two simple calculations, I got a pretty decent $\\pi$ accurate to $10$ digits after the decimal point.  If I had the data on the circumscribed polygons, I suppose the # of accurate digits would increase even more.  It makes sense that there is predictability since we are doubling the # of sides each time and not some random increase like from $6$ to $10$ to $25$ sides.</p>\n\n<p>So in my extrapolation method, my first step would be to take the last known \"delta\" of $.00321190181017$ and \"quarter\" it to $0.00080297545254$ so that the next tuple entry would be (extrapolation 1, $3.14145247226851\n, 0.00042052137800, 3.99919702454746, 0.00080297545254$)</p>\n\n<p>Also it is worth mentioning that using only data from up to $24$ inscribed polygons, my extrapolation method yields a quite good approximation of $3.14159265308398$ which is accurate to $9$ places after the decimal and that is as good as using about a $100,000$ sided polygon using Archimedes method.</p>\n\n<p>So does anyone know if there is documentation that Archimedes knew about this extrapolation method (or similar) during his lifetime? </p>\n\n<p><strong>UPDATE:</strong></p>\n\n<p>I revisited my spreadsheet and got a more accurate version of $ \\pi$ using this method. Notice how the previous estimate was on the low side of the actual value of $\\pi$ but the new estimate is slightly on the high side.</p>\n\n<p>Also, here is the follow up YouTube video link for the Archimedes method of circumscribed polygon to \"sandwich\" the value of $\\pi$ between.  With those two values, the confidence level of $\\pi$ goes way up. <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=9zO0-QOcJQ0\" rel=\"nofollow\">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=9zO0-QOcJQ0</a></p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>This is not an answer, just an alternative exposition of your findings as far as I was able to reproduce them.</p>\n\n<p>First, the video describes Archimedes' method for approximating $\\pi$ via the following recurrence for the side length of a regular $3\\cdot2^n$-gon inscribed in a circle, which I'll call $\\ell_n$:\n$$ \\ell_1=1 \\text{ ,}\\qquad \\ell_{n+1} = 2\\sqrt{2-\\sqrt{4-\\ell_n^2}} \\tag1 $$\nThen we approximate $\\pi$ as half the perimeter of that $3\\cdot2^n$-gon, that is,\n$$ h_n = 3\\cdot 2^{n-1} \\ell_n $$</p>\n\n<p>What you've noticed numerically is that\n$$ \\frac{h_n-h_{n-1}}{h_{n-1}-h_{n-2}} \\approx \\frac14 \\tag2 $$\n(Nice observation, by the way.  If you do the trigonometry, it turns out that\n$$ \\frac{h_n-h_{n-1}}{h_{n-1}-h_{n-2}} = \\frac1{2\\cos\\theta_n(1+\\cos\\theta_n)}\n\\text{ ,}\\quad\\text{ where } \\theta_n = \\frac{\\pi}{3\\cdot2^n} $$\nwhich confirms your finding because $\\cos\\theta\\to 1$ as $\\theta\\to 0$.  This also shows that the LHS is always $\\ge\\frac14$.)</p>\n\n<p>Next, you started approximating $h_n$ using (2) instead of the Archimedean recurrence (1).  Actually, you say you have something more precise than $\\frac14$ on the RHS of (2), but you don't say what it is, so <strong>I'll just proceed as if you used $\\frac14$.</strong></p>\n\n<p>Let's call the values obtained by this method $\\hat h_n$.  Since you started using this method after the $96$-gon, the sequence is given by the rules\n$$ \\hat h_4 = h_4 \\text{ ,}\\quad \\hat h_5 = h_5 \\text{ ,}\\quad \\frac{\\hat h_n-\\hat h_{n-1}}{\\hat h_{n-1}-\\hat h_{n-2}} = \\frac14 $$\nBy telescoping product, we get\n$$ \\hat h_n - \\hat h_{n-1} = (\\hat h_5 - \\hat h_4) \\prod_{k=6}^n \\frac{\\hat h_k-\\hat h_{k-1}}{\\hat h_{k-1}-\\hat h_{k-2}}\n= \\frac{\\hat h_5 - \\hat h_4}{4^{n-5}} $$\nand then, by telescoping sum,\n$$ \\hat h_n = \\hat h_5 + \\sum_{k=6}^n (\\hat h_k-\\hat h_{k-1})\n= \\hat h_5 + (\\hat h_5-\\hat h_4)\\sum_{k=6}^n \\frac1{4^{k-5}}\n= \\hat h_5 + \\frac13 (\\hat h_5-\\hat h_4) \\Big(1-\\frac1{4^{n-4}}\\Big)\n$$\nNow you can see that as $n\\to\\infty$, we have\n$$ \\hat h_n \\to \\hat h_5 + \\frac13 (\\hat h_5-\\hat h_4)\n= \\frac{4h_5-h_4}{3}\n\\approx 3.1415925335\n$$\nSo, <em>this</em> sequence is not actually tending to $\\pi$, but to something a bit smaller.</p>\n\n<p><strong>Update:</strong> I don't know whether Archimedes knew your method, but I will note that the summation of the infinite geometric series used here was absolutely something he knew — he did that as part of his computation of the area of a parabolic sector.</p>\n\n<p><strong>Update:</strong> It occurs to me that another way to use your idea is to compute $h_n$ via (1) as usual, but rather than report that as your estimate, report $(4h_n-h_{n-1})/3$.  This is as if you had used (2) starting from $n$ instead of starting from $6$, and it gives a sharper lower bound for $\\pi$.  (It's a lower bound because, as noted above, in (2) we have $\\ge$.)  You still have to use (1), unfortunately.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/1000916/archimedes-trisection-of-equilateral-triangle","title":"Archimedes trisection of equilateral triangle?","creationDate":1414823533,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":1000916,"body":"<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/XDIfO.jpg\" alt=\"Original Equilateral Triangle\"></p>\n\n<p>Equilateral triangle paradox </p>\n\n<p>Consider triangle  ABC: Each side is trisected - AE, FC, GB each = 1/3 of their sides.</p>\n\n<p>First we established that triangle HIJ was equilateral. This was accomplished rather simply by establishing ACE=CBF=BAG via SAS; then the smaller triangles FCH=EIA=GJB via ASA. Using the vertical angle rule, we can establish that HIJ is equilateral.</p>\n\n<p>The second challenge was to show that area of triangle HIJ = 1/7 the area of triangle ABC. While I would have liked to do this strictly through proof, I gave up like the rest of our group and resorted to establishing arbitrary coordinates (i.e. A = (0,0); B = (6,0)), to calculate the areas of each congruent triangle AJB, BHC, and CIA. These came to equal 2/7 each. Multiplied by three of them = 6/7, leaving 1/7 remaining area for HIJ.</p>\n\n<p>I was disappointed that we had to resort to this method. I would prefer a generalized geometric proof. If anyone could illuminate a path for that if it is possible, that would be great.</p>\n\n<p>Once again utilizing arbitrary values given to the triangle, I also used the law of cosines to determine measure of AG (and therefore CE,BF). From here, standard stuff with law of sines to determine angles of triangle AGB (and their congruent buddies). Same fashion for AIE and the congruent triangles there. Thus area of HIJ = area ABC - area 3AGB - area 3AIE. Ta da! 1/7 (or suuuuper really close)</p>\n\n<p>Geometry says that with GB being 1/3 of CB, GAB cannot be exactly 1/3 of CAB.(it actually works out to approx. 19.11 degrees using the methods I describe above - any rays trisecting a vertex of an equilateral triangle going through trisection points of opposite sides create approx. 19.11/21.78\\19.11 set of angles at each vertex) Also, we know this because trisection of an angle can only create three equal distances along the arc of the circle made from a radius AB.</p>\n\n<p>But,the strange thing is that looking at just straight edge and compass Archimedes method of proving 1/3 of an angle which determines that IAE should be 20 degrees almost appeared to work. </p>\n\n<p>I applied this method to my triangle ABC by using a compass to measure length AI. Pivoting about point I, the arc intersects AB and here I mark origin O. From origin O, I add a line parallel to AC that intersects AB at origin point O. I then followed Archimedes method as shown here:  <a href=\"http://mathschallenge.net/library/constructions/trisecting_angle\" rel=\"nofollow\">http://mathschallenge.net/library/constructions/trisecting_angle</a></p>\n\n<p>At first, I thought I had found some paradox! Nevertheless, it enables us to demonstrate Bolzano’s theorem by\nvirtue of which, for all sets of points situated upon a straight line between two definite\npoints of the same line, there exists necessarily a point of condensation, that is to say,\na limiting point. From a theoretical point of view, the value of this axiom is that it\nleads indirectly to the introduction of limiting points, and, hence, renders it possible to\nestablish a one-to-one correspondence between the points of a segment and the system\nof real numbers.</strong></p>\n\n<p>These are the definitions I'm talking about: </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <blockquote>\n    <p><strong>Hilbert's Axiom of completeness:</strong> To a system of points, straight lines, and planes, it is impossible to add other elements in such a manner that the system thus generalized shall form a new geometry obeying all of the five groups of axioms. In other words, the elements of geometry form a system which is not susceptible of extension, if we regard the five groups of axioms as valid.</p>\n    \n    <p><strong>Dedekind's Axiom:</strong> Suppose that the set ${l}$ of all points on a line $l$ is the disjoint union $\\Sigma_1\\cup \\Sigma_2$ of two nonempty subsets such that no point of either subset is between two points of the other.Then there exists a unique point $o$ on $l$ such that one of the subsets is equal to a ray of $l$ with vertex $o$ and the other subset is equal to the complement.</p>\n    \n    <p><strong>Cantor's Axiom:</strong> Let $\\{\\overline{A_nB_n}\\}_{n\\in N}$ be a sequence of segments on\n    some straight line such that $\\overline{A_{n+1}B_{n+1}}\\subset \\overline{A_{n}B_{n}}$ for all $n\\in \\mathbb{N}$. Then the intersection of such segments is not empty and there is\n    a point $o$ that belongs to all of them.</p>\n    \n    <p><strong>Archimedes' Axiom:</strong> For any two segments $\\overline{AB}$ and $\\overline{OE}$ there is\n    a positive integer $n\\in \\mathbb{N}$ such that $\\overline{AB}&lt;n\\cdot\\overline{OE}$.</p>\n  </blockquote>\n</blockquote>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Hilbert's completeness axiom is not a standard axiom because it is about the other axioms, it is rather a meta-axiom about the models of the other axioms.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/734805/multiplicative-version-of-the-principle-of-archimedes","title":"Multiplicative version of the principle of Archimedes","creationDate":1396329921,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":734805,"body":"<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/i17Tn.jpg\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></p>\n\n<p>Any clear proof of the above theorem is greatly appreciated.</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Suppose first that $y\\ge 1$. We prove that the set $S=\\{x^n\\}$, $n=0,1,2,\\dots$ is unbounded. Suppose to the contrary that $S$ bounded. Let $b$ be the supremum. Then for some $n$, we have $x^n \\gt \\frac{b}{(x+1)/2}$. Then $x^{n+1}\\gt b\\frac{x}{(x+1)/2}\\gt b$. This contradicts the fact that $b$ is an upper bound of $S$. </p>\n\n<p>Thus the set of positive integers $k$ such that $x^k\\gt y$ is non-empty. Let $n$ be the smallest element of this set. Then $x^{n-1}\\le y$, and we are finished.</p>\n\n<p>For $0\\lt y\\lt 1$, the easiest way to proceed is to show that there is a non-negative  integer $m$ such that $x^m \\lt \\frac{1}{y}\\le x^{m+1}$. The argument for this is almost the same as the previous one. </p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/720935/historic-proof-of-the-area-of-a-circle","title":"Historic proof of the area of a circle","creationDate":1395391469,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":720935,"body":"<p>The area of a circle radius $R$ is $\\pi R^2$ which is quite easy to prove with integral calculus.</p>\n\n<p>Consider a ring of radius $\\mathrm{d}r$ at a distance $r$ from the centre. This ring has area $2\\pi r \\mathrm{d}r$. </p>\n\n<p>Integrating, </p>\n\n<p>$$\\int_0^R2\\pi r \\mathrm\\,{d}r=\\pi R^2$$</p>\n\n<p>But calculus is a relatively new tool, while the area of a circle has been known for what I presume since Archimedes at least. So what is the historic derivation for this? </p>\n\n<p>I think it is something related to the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Method_of_exhaustion/\" rel=\"nofollow\">method of exhaustion</a> which is in essence a primitive form of integration, the Wikipedia article states</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Archimedes used the method of exhaustion as a way to compute the area inside a circle by filling the circle with a polygon of a greater area and greater number of sides. The quotient formed by the area of this polygon divided by the square of the circle radius can be made arbitrarily close to $π$ as the number of polygon sides becomes large, proving that the area inside the circle of radius $r$ is $πr^2$, $π$ being defined as the ratio of the circumference to the diameter $\\frac{C}{d}$ or of the area of the circle to the square of its radius $A/r^2$.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>I am unclear as to how Archimedes would have proved that these two definitions of $\\pi$ are in fact equivalent and refer to the same constant. Maybe in his proof of $\\pi r^2$ the equality emerges but I have been unable to find any reference to or a description of how Archimedes proved this.</p>\n\n<p>I don't know what to tag this, apart from reference request, so feel free to re-tag.</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>The proof is straightforward.</p>\n\n<p>1) You need to know that similar figures have constant ratios between corresponding lengths, so call the ratio between a circle's circumference and its diameter pi. In other words, the circumference = 2.pi.r where r is the radius (half the diameter)</p>\n\n<p>2) If you construct a polygon inside the circle it can be viewed as a number of triangles arranged round the centre of the circle. The area of each triangle is 1/2 .base length.r' (where r' is the perpendicular height) and if you add up all the areas you have 1/2 .perimiter length.r' </p>\n\n<p>3) As the polygon gets more sides then it gets closer to the circle so that in the limit the area of the polygon is the area of the circle, r' = r, and the perimiter is the circumference of the circle.</p>\n\n<p>4) Putting everything together, the perimiter = circumference = 2.pi.r and the area is then 1/2 . 2.pi.r. r, i.e. pi.r^2</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/689171/what-problems-are-there-left-to-solve","title":"What problems are there left to solve?","creationDate":1393284658,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":689171,"body":"<p>From the ancient Greek mathematicians (Archimedes, Pythagoras) before Christ to Issac Newton to George Birkhoff, these mathematicians have made huge strides in mathematics, developing theorems and even entire mathematical systems. But these theorems and systems were created with a objective in mind, a problem to be solved. Nowadays, asides from a few theorems to be proved (such as the Millennium Problems) it seems that there are no problems which haven't been solved or haven't had an algorithm for solving the problem developed. \nWhile I consider Mathematics a noble and virtuous pursuit, comparable to Physics or Medicine, where can modern mathematics go? What problems are there to solve? What physical or abstract problems have been left unsolved?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>I have come to think of mathematics as an ever growing tree. The leaves are the open questions, and the branches are the already existing theorems; the more important and older the theorem, the thickest the branch. Later, <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johan_Ludvig_Heiberg_%28historian%29\" rel=\"nofollow\">Johan Heiberg</a> noticed that this is closely related to an axiom appearing in Euclid V.4. The term has become commonly accepted since then. It should be noted that none of the European mathematicians from <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simon_Stevin\" rel=\"nofollow\">Simon Stevin</a> and until the end of the 19th century used the term in this sense, so in a way the commonly accepted term \"archimedean field\" is somewhat ahistorical, which does not detract from its usefulness. You have your geometry, all of which (taught in high school) was known to the Greeks more than 2 thousand years ago. You have medieval trigonometry and algebra. You also have 17th century calculus, and that's about it for most high schools. </p>\n\n<p>Now for somebody seriously interested in mathematics, it is hard to decide where to continue your studies after completeling the high school curriculum. Of course you could just start reading about a certain field you are particularly interested in and disregard the other fields as much as you can, but this might give you gaps in your knowledge. You could just learn more advanced calculus, like people usually do, but you'll get the same dilemma after finishing multivariable calculus. </p>\n\n<p>So what I've been doing is reading about the history of mathematics and just reading the major developments in chronological order. Pre-Greeks there was some mathematics, but almost all of it is extremely trivial for a high schooler, and there are no proofs, so the Greeks is where I started. What I'd then do is just read some of their works very selectively, just the most revolutionary stuff. As an aspiring physicist, the 17th century is just where it begins to get complicated and I was wondering if it would be smart to keep on doing it like this up until around the 20th century. In my opinion, it flavours up the learning process, it is much more fun than reading a boring text book, it also shows the motivation, and of course reading from the original author themselves in some cases is beneficial (and if it isn't, there are a billion explanations online).</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>There is little intrinsic value to studying mathematics chronologically, unless you are interested in the history of mathematics, as opposed to purely mathematics itself. You reasoned that learning the history of physics is interesting and can help 'flavor' the learning. Of course, the history of physics is not the same as that of mathematics -- I would consider the history of physics a little more interesting. </p>\n\n<p>It is reasonable to study mathematics by moving gradually from the basics to the more advanced topics. It makes sense that this naturally carries you throughout the ages, as the basic ideas are older (at least in part) -- but there have been important revisions even to basic mathematics in recent history (for example, Zermelo-Fraenkel Set Theory). If you wish to simply build a good understanding of mathematics, then I suggest you disregard the history and simply focus on learning the math itself.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/593421/is-there-a-proof-for-archimedes-pictorial-proof-for-the-approximation-of-pi","title":"Is there a proof for Archimedes&#39; pictorial proof for the approximation of pi?","creationDate":1386204566,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":593421,"body":"<p>Is there a proof for Archimedes' pictorial proof for the approximation of pi? If not, please correct me.  </p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>The difference between supremum and maximum is that a maximum is in your set, while a supremum is the smallest upper bound. (Definition of Convergence)</li>\n<li>A sequence $(a'_k)_{k≥1}$ is called a subsequence of a sequence $(a_n)_{n≥1}$, if there is a monotonous increasing sequence $(n_k)_{k≥1}\\in\\mathbb{N}$ so that $a'_{k} = a_{n_{k}}$ for $\\forall k≥1$. (Cauchy\nCriterion).</p>\n\n<p>You wrote it yourself.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/557604/show-that-0-1-is-open-in-mathbbr-and-that-0-1-is-not-open-in-math","title":"Show that $(0,1)$ is open in $\\mathbb{R}$ and that $[0,1]$ is not open in $\\mathbb{R}$","creationDate":1383970048,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":557604,"body":"<p><strong>My Question:</strong> I want to show that the interval $(0,1)$ is open in $\\mathbb{R}$  and that $[0,1]$ is not open in $\\mathbb{R}$. I proved the latter, but I felt that it was clumsy and could be refined. If you could also show that $(0,1)$ is open that would be great; these are the simplest problems that I can think of, and I am concerned with the format of the proof (my intuition tells me that they are both true). I am working with the following definitions (I may have made an error while translating the definitions into symbolic logic, if so please correct me). Thanks!</p>\n\n<p><strong>Definitions:</strong>\nA set $G$\n  in $\\mathbb{R}^{p}$\n  is said to be <strong>open in</strong> $\\mathbb{R}^{p}$\n  if for all $x$\n  in $G$\n  ,there exists an $r&gt;0$\n  , such that for each $y$\n  in $\\mathbb{R}^{p}$\n  satisfying the condition $\\|x-y\\|&lt;r$\n  also belong to $G$.\n  Or, even more formally we could write $$G\\text{ is an }\\textbf{open set} \\iff G\\subset\\mathbb{R}^{p}\\wedge\\forall x\\in G\\exists r&gt;0\\in\\mathbb{R}\\left(\\forall y\\in\\mathbb{R}^{p}\\left(\\|x-y\\|&lt;r\\iff y\\in G\\right)\\right).$$</p>\n\n<p>Using the definition of an open ball $\\mathcal{B}(x,r)=\\{y\\in\\mathbb{R}^{p}\\::\\:\\|x-y\\|&lt;r\\}$\n , where $x$\n  is the center of the ball and $r&gt;0$\n  is the radius of the ball, then we can rephrase the definition of an <strong>open set</strong> by saying that $G$\n  is open if every point $x$\n  in $G$\n  is the center of some open ball contained entirely in $G.$  Again more formally we can write </p>\n\n<p>$$G \\text{ is an }\\textbf{open set}\\iff G\\subset\\mathbb{R}^{p}\\wedge\\forall x\\in G\\exists r&gt;0\\in\\mathbb{R}\\left(\\mathcal{B}(x,r)\\subset G\\right).$$</p>\n\n<p><strong>My work:</strong></p>\n\n<p>Consider the set $F=\\{x\\in\\mathbb{R}\\::\\:0\\leqslant x\\leqslant1\\}\\subset\\mathbb{R}$\n , is $F$\n  an open set? </p>\n\n<p>No! <em>proof.</em> Since, we know that $G$\n  is a subset of $\\mathbb{R}^{p}$\n , we need only consider the second conjunct in the bi-conditional above. The negation of the statement $\\forall x\\in G\\exists r&gt;0\\in\\mathbb{R}\\left(\\mathcal{B}(x,r)\\subset G\\right)$\n  is, $\\exists x\\in G\\forall r&gt;0\\in\\mathbb{R}\\left(\\mathcal{B}(x,r)\\not\\subset G\\right)$\n . </p>\n\n<p>Now let us consider the point $x=1$ in $F$\n then we must consider all of the balls centered at $1$ with a radius $r&gt;0$, i.e. ${\\cal B}(1,r)=\\{y\\in\\mathbb{R}\\::\\:|1-y|&lt;r\\}$. If we want to show that the negation is false then we need to find a ball such that for each $y\\in{\\cal B}(1,r)$, $y\\in G$. However, by the Archimedes property we have that we know that for all $r&gt;0$\n  that there will always be a number $y^*=1/n$ such that  $0&lt;y^*&lt;r$\n , and that $(1+y^*)\\in{\\cal B}(1,r)$\n  while $(1+y^*)\\notin G$\n . Therefore given the element $1$\n  in $F$\n , ${\\cal B}(1,r)\\not\\subset G$\n  for all $r&gt;0$\n ; hence, $F$\n  is not an open set.</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Yes, you've got exactly the right idea: Any ball centered at $1$ must contain a number greater than $1$. For a more direct proof that's constructive (as opposed to just invoking the Archimdean property), consider:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Suppose that $[0, 1]$ is open. Then there exists an $r &gt; 0$ such that whenever $|y - 1| &lt; r$, we have $y \\in [0, 1]$. Choose $$y = 1 + \\frac{r}{2}$$\n  This leads to a contradiction since $|y - 1| = |r/2| &lt; r$ but $y \\notin [0, 1]$. </p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p>As a hint to show that $(0, 1)$ is open, let $x \\in (0, 1)$ be given. Consider $$r = \\frac{\\min(x, 1 - x)}{2}$$\nand show that that $\\mathcal{B}(x, r) \\subseteq (0, 1)$.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/542870/did-euclid-prove-that-pi-is-constant","title":"Did Euclid prove that $\\pi$ is constant?","creationDate":1382971081,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":542870,"body":"<p>Pi is defined the ratio of the circumference of a circle to its diameter, but of course different circles have different circumferences and diameters, so in order for it to be well-defined we need to show that the ratios for any two circles is the same.  This is fairly trivial if we approximate the circles by regular n-gons and take the limit as n goes to infinity, what the ancients called Eudoxus' method of exhaustion.  Archimedes used this method with great success, in finding the circumference and area of a circle, the volume and surface area of a sphere, area bounded by a parabola, etc.</p>\n\n<p>My question is, did Euclid ever prove that Pi is constant in his Elements?  In <a href=\"http://aleph0.clarku.edu/~djoyce/java/elements/bookXII/propXII2.html\">Book XII Proposition II</a>, he proves that the ratio of the area of a circle to the square of its diameter is the same for all circles, but does he ever prove that the ratio of a circumference of a circle to its diameter is the same for all circles?  In <a href=\"http://aleph0.clarku.edu/~djoyce/java/elements/bookVI/propVI33.html\">Book VI Proposition 33</a> he proves that for two circles of equal diameter, the length of an arc is proportional to the angle that subtends it, but does he ever relate the lengths of arcs on unequal circles to each other?</p>\n\n<p>Even if Euclid didn't prove this result, is it at least an easy corollary of something he did prove?</p>\n\n<p>Any help would be greatly appreciated.</p>\n\n<p>Thank You in Advance.</p>\n\n<p>EDIT:  In <a href=\"http://mathoverflow.net/a/72883/5017\">this thread on MathOverflow</a>, it's claimed that the result follows immediately from <a href=\"http://aleph0.clarku.edu/~djoyce/java/elements/bookIII/propIII34.html\">Book III Proposition 34</a> and <a href=\"http://aleph0.clarku.edu/~djoyce/java/elements/bookVI/propVI33.html\">Book VI Proposition 33</a>, but I don't see how it follows at all. As I said above, Book VI Proposition 33 is about arc lengths for circles of equal diameter, so how do you get from that to a result about arc lengths for circles of unequal diameter? Book III Proposition 34, which is just about transferring angles from one circle to another, doesn't seem like it would suffice.</p>\n\n<p>EDIT 2:  I think there's a proposition that the result is even more likely to follow from than Book VI Proposition 33: <a href=\"http://aleph0.clarku.edu/~djoyce/java/elements/bookIII/propIII27.html\">Book III Proposition 27</a>, which says that equal arcs on equal circles correspond to equal angles.  Is there any way to use that proposition to prove that arcs on two UNequal circles corresponding to equal angles are proportional to the diameters of the circles?  That is to say, if S1 and S2 are arcs subtended by equal angles on circles of diameter D1 and D2 respectively, then S1/S2 = D1/D2.</p>\n\n<p>EDIT 3:  I should make clear that Euclid may not have viewed \"the ratio of a circumference of a circle to its diameter\" as meaningful, but I think he would have found meaningful the statement I gave in my previous edit: if S1 and S2 are arcs subtended by equal angles on circles of diameter D1 and D2 respectively, then the ratio of S1 to S2 is equal to the ratio of D1 to D2.  I should also mention that Euclid's definition of the equality of two ratios is <a href=\"http://aleph0.clarku.edu/~djoyce/java/elements/bookV/defV5.html\">Eudoxus' theory of proportion</a>, a precursor to the Dedekind cut construction of the real numbers.</p>\n\n<p>EDIT 4:  It occurs to me that just as Euclid believed that a straight line and a circular arc were not magnitudes of the same kind, he may have believed the same thing about circular arcs in circles of unequal diameter, i.e. he may have thought that it's meaningless to ask whether a circular arc from one circle is longer or shorter than a circular arc from another circle if the circles don't have the same diameter.  Can anyone confirm or deny this?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>There is a proof that pi is well-defined as ratio of circumference to diameter in the text by E. Moise \"Elementary Geometry from an Advanced Standpoint\"; he applies results on similarity from Book VI to the polygons approximating the circle.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/537897/calculus-question-about-finding-a-limit-of-a-sequence-of-functions","title":"calculus question about finding a limit of a sequence of functions.","creationDate":1382595479,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":537897,"body":"<p>Let $$f_n = n \\chi_{[0,\\frac{1}{n} ]}$$</p>\n\n<p>I want to find $ \\lim f_n$. I think it is $0$ since if we fix $x$ then can find $x &gt; \\frac{1}{N} $ By archimedes. So $f_n(x) $ must be $0$ for all $n &gt; N$ is this correct?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>It seems like $f_n(x)$ approaches $0$ for each positive $x$, is $0$ for any negative $x$, and approaches $\\infty$ for $x=0$. Significant, simply in not being just a translation into geometric form, as in Venn Diagrams for boolean algebras (first being represented as some system of sets), but that allows for something deeper to be said about logic itself?</p>\n\n<p>There is of course the Stone representation for a Boolean Algebra, but what does this significantly say about logic?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>One direction you <em>could</em> explore is mapped out e.g. by Saunders Mac Lane and Ieke Moerdijk's <em>Sheaves in Geometry and Logic: A First Introduction to Topos Theory</em> (Springer, 1992). Their Prologue begins</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>A startling aspect of topos theory is that it unifies two seemingly\n  wholly distinct mathematical subjects: on the one hand, topology and\n  algebraic geometry, and on the other hand, logic and set theory. Indeed,\n  a topos can be considered both as a \"generalized space\" and as a \n  \"generalized universe of sets\" ...</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Another direction you could explore is Homotopy Type Theory -- here is an introductory paper by Steve Awodey: <a href=\"http://www.andrew.cmu.edu/user/awodey/preprints/TTH.pdf\" rel=\"nofollow\">http://www.andrew.cmu.edu/user/awodey/preprints/TTH.pdf</a>  His opening words are</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>The purpose of this informal survey article is to introduce the reader\n  to a new and surprising connection between Geometry, Algebra, and\n  Logic, which has recently come to light in the form of an interpretation\n  of the constructive type theory of Per Martin-Löf into homotopy\n  theory, ...</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>So those are two initial pointers to areas where deep geometry/logic connections are indeed made. </p>\n\n<p>But -- and it is rather a big 'but'! -- these are mathematical investigations at a level of conceptual sophistication and difficulty that go far beyond that of introductory mathematical logic. By assumption $p$ is the largest prime, so $p!+1$ cannot be prime. Hence it has a prime divisor $q|p!+1$. But $q\\le p$, so $q|p!$. From this we conclude $q|p!+1-p!=1$, which is absurd. If the assumption that there is a largest prime leads to a contradiction, then the assumption must be false and there is no largest prime.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/489871/infinite-amount-of-additions-finite-sum","title":"Infinite amount of additions, finite sum?","creationDate":1378845526,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":489871,"body":"<p>I suggest it's a popular question, so if it was asked already (I couldn't find it anyway), close this question instead of downvoting, thanks!</p>\n\n<p>Let's check this addition: $\\sum_{n=0}^{\\infty}\\frac{1}{2^n}=2$</p>\n\n<p>It looks like $1 + \\frac12 + \\frac14 + \\frac18 + \\frac1{16} + ... $ for every natural $n$.</p>\n\n<p>How can the sum of this (and other similar) additions be finite? For example\n$$\\lim_{n\\to\\infty} \\sum_{k=1}^n \\frac{1}{k^2} = \\frac{\\pi^2}{6}&lt;\\infty$$\nBut\n$$\\lim_{n\\to\\infty} \\sum_{k=1}^n \\frac{1}{k} = \\infty$$\nSo for the convergence of series (which is what you are puzzled about), there are a lot of so-called convergence tests for the summands $a_k$ to determine, whether\n$$\\lim_{n\\to\\infty} s_n := \\lim_{n\\to\\infty} \\sum_{k=1}^n a_k$$\nconverges.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://math.stackexchange.com/questions/487051/why-cant-the-alpertron-solve-this-pell-like-equation","title":"Why can&#39;t the Alpertron solve this Pell-like equation?","creationDate":1378609717,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"math","questionID":487051,"body":"<p>Dario Alpern's <a href=\"http://www.alpertron.com.ar/QUAD.HTM\" rel=\"nofollow\"><em>Alpertron</em></a> is convenient for solving Pell and Pell-like equations.
