","creationDate":1313180454,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"mathoverflow","questionID":72792,"body":"<p>I have an elementary question about the history of $\\pi$. I thought the answer would be easy to find. But, to the contrary, after quite a bit of searching and after consulting math historians, I have been unable to find a satisfactory answer.</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Who first <em>proved</em> that $C/d$ is independent of the choice of circle ($C$ and $d$ are the circumference and diameter, respectively)?</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Or equivalently: </p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Who first proved that given two circles with circumferences $C_1$ and $C_2$ and diameters $d_1$ and $d_2$, that $C_1/C_2=d_1/d_2$? (Or, as I imagine Euclid would have written it: the circumferences of circles are to one another as their diameters.)</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Most accounts of the history of $\\pi$ spend a lot of time talking about how this fact has been \"known\" for a long time (giving Egyptian, Babylonian, biblical, etc. approximations to the value). But they never say who first proved it. I expected it to be in Euclid's <em>Elements</em>, but was surprised to find that it isn't. Can I take that to mean that it hadn't been proved by then? I would be very surprised if the proof was known to Euclid and he had not included it in <em>Elements.</em></p>\n\n<p>Note: Euclid does contain Eudoxus's proposition that $A_1/A_2=d_1^2/d_2^2$, where the $A_i$ are the areas of the two circles (<em>Elements</em> XII.2: Circles are to one another as the squares on their diameters.). This implies that the value of $A/d^2$ is independent of the choice of circle. </p>\n\n<p>If we jump ahead a few years from Euclid we find the fact that $C/d$ is constant given implicitly in Archimedes's <em>Measurement of the Circle</em>. First of all, he finds bounds for $C/d$ (it being between $223/71$ and $22/7$). So presumably he knew that it was a constant. But also, it follows logically from his result that $A=rC/2$, where $r$ is the radius of the circle (Archimedes says that the area of a circle is equal to the area of a triangle with height $r$ and base $C$): if we take Eudoxus's proposition as saying $A=kd^2$ (for some constant $k$) and Archimedes's result as $A=dC/4$, then setting them equal we get $kd^2=dC/4$, or equivalently $C/d=4k$ (i.e., $k=\\pi/4$).</p>\n\n<p>So, my question is: who first prove this fact? Was it Archimedes? I've read that the version of the <em>Measurement of the Circle</em> that we have may be only a part of what Archimedes actually wrote. Do people conjecture that it was proved and stated explicitly in the missing part of this document?</p>\n\n<p>This all seems very mysterious to me. I would be a little surprised to discover that the answer to this question is lost to history since it is such a major mathematical result (but maybe that is so). I would be surprised if it took until Archimedes to get a proof of this; if it was \"known\" empirically for the entire Greek period (which I assume it was), one would imagine that a rigorous proof would be highly sought after. One imagines a proof would have been within Eudoxus's reach. Finally, whether the answer the answer to the question is known or not known, I have been very surprised that no one has written about this fact (or at least not that I've found).</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>I think this has to do a lot with what you mean by proof.  In particular, how are you defining the arclength of a circle without calculus?</p>\n\n<p>For me, it seems hard to say that anyone proved $C/d$ is independent of the choice of circle before Newton and Leibniz.  Once the framework of integration is in place, however, this fact is a triviality, and doesn't seem like a great advance at the time calculus was invented.  The great advance was rigorously defining the length of a parameterized arc.</p>\n\n<p>Of course, Archimedes famous theorem about the ratio between the volumes of a sphere and a circumscribing cylinder was essentially proved using integration, although Archimedes didn't have the framework of a limit to phrase the argument.  If you're satisfied with that type of reasoning, then perhaps the answer is different.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://mathoverflow.net/questions/67384/source-and-context-of-frac227-pi-int-01-x-x24-dx-1x2","title":"Source and context of $\\frac{22}{7} - \\pi = \\int_0^1 (x-x^2)^4 dx/(1+x^2)$?","creationDate":1307655821,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"mathoverflow","questionID":67384,"body":"<p>Possibly the most striking proof of Archimedes's inequality $\\pi &lt; 22/7$ is an integral formula for the difference:\n$$\n\\frac{22}{7} - \\pi = \\int_0^1 (x-x^2)^4 \\frac{dx}{1+x^2},\n$$\nwhere the integrand is manifestly positive.  This formula is \"well-known\" but its origin remains somewhat mysterious.  I ask:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Who discovered this integral, and in what context?</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>The earliest reference I know of is Problem A-1 on the 29th Putnam Exam (1968).  According to J.H.McKay's report in the <em>American Math. Monthly</em> (Vol.76 (1969) #8, 909-915), the Questions Committee consisted of N.D.Kazarinoff, Leo Moser, and Albert Wilansky.  Is one of them the discoverer, and if so which one?</p>\n\n<p>The printed solution, both in the <em>Monthly</em> article and in the book by Klosinski, Alexanderson, and Larson, says only \"The standard approach, from elementary calculus, applies. By division, rewrite the integrand as a polynomial plus a rational function with numerator of degree less than 2. The solution follows easily.\"  But surely there's more to be said, because this integral is a minor miracle of mathematics:</p>\n\n<p>$\\bullet$ Not only is the integrand manifestly positive, but it is always small: $x-x^2 \\in [0,1/4]$ for $x \\in [0,1]$, and the denominator $1+x^2$ is at least 1, so $(x-x^2)^4/(x^2+1) &lt; 1/4^4 = 1/256$.  A better upper bound on the integral is $\\int_0^1 (x-x^2)^4 dx$, which comes to $1/630$ either by direct expansion or by recognizing the Beta integral $B(5,5)=4!^2/9!$.  Hence $\\frac{22}{7} - \\pi &lt; 1/630$, which also yields Archimedes's lower bound $\\pi &gt; 3\\frac{10}{71}$.</p>\n\n<p>$\\bullet$ The \"standard approach\" explains how to evaluate the integral, but not why the answer is so simple.  When we expand\n$$\n\\frac{(x-x^2)^4}{1+x^2} = x^6 - 4x^5 + 5x^4 - 4x^2 + 4 - \\frac4{x^2+1},\n$$\nthe coefficient of $x/(x^2+1)$ vanishes, so there's no $\\log 2$ term in the integral.  [This much I can understand: the numerator $(x-x^2)^4$ takes the same value $(1\\pm i)^4 = -4$ at both roots of the denominator $x^2+1$.]  When we integrate the polynomial part, we might\nexpect to combine fractions with denominators of 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, and 7, obtaining a complicated rational number.  But only 7 appears: there's no $x$ or $x^3$ term; the $x^4$ coefficient 5 kills the denominator of 5; and the terms $-4x^5-4x^2$ might have contributed denominators of 6 and 3 combine to yield the integer $-1$.</p>\n\n<p>Compare this with the next such integrals\n$$\n\\int_0^1 (x-x^2)^6 \\frac{dx}{1+x^2} = \\frac{38429}{13860} - 4 \\log 2\n$$\nand\n$$\n\\int_0^1 (x-x^2)^8 \\frac{dx}{1+x^2} = 4\\pi - \\frac{188684}{15015},\n$$\nwhich yield better but much more complicated approximations to $\\log 2$ and $\\pi$...</p>\n\n<p>This suggests a refinement of the \"in what context\" part of the question:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Does that integral for $(22/7)-\\pi$ generalize to give further approximations to $\\pi$ (or $\\log 2$ or similar constants) that are useful for the study of Diophantine properties of $\\pi$ (or $\\log 2$ etc.)?</p>\n</blockquote>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Jonathan M. Borwein, David H. Bailey and Roland Girgensohn discuss this and related formulae for $\\pi$ in their book <em>\"Experimentation in Mathematics\"</em> (see Section 1.1, p. 3). They claim that</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>The integral was apparently shown by Kurt Mahler to his students in\n  the mid-1960s, and it had appeared in a mathematical examination at the\n  University of Sydney in November, 1960.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>They mention also a paper by Beuker who further developed the method of integral representations to obtain the irrationality estimate \n$$\\left|\\pi-\\frac{p}{q}\\right|\\geq\\frac{1}{q^{21.04...}},$$<br>\nwhich holds true for all integers $p$, $q$ with sufficiently large $q$. The exponent $21.04...$ is rather far from being optimal. </p>\n\n<p><a href=\"http://carma.newcastle.edu.au/~jb616/Preprints/Books/MbyE/expbook-II.pdf\">A draft</a> of the book is freely available on J.M. Borwein's home page.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://mathoverflow.net/questions/54393/analytical-solutions-of-a-differential-equation-from-archimedes-spiral","title":"Analytical solutions of a differential equation (from Archimedes&#39; Spiral) ","creationDate":1296901335,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"mathoverflow","questionID":54393,"body":"<p>There is a differential equation in polar coordinates:\n$r'^2+r^2=(kt)^2$, $r(t=0)=0$, k- Const.</p>\n\n<p>I've found that\na) if $\\phi \\in (0,t)$, t is quite small, then $r(\\phi) \\approx k/2 *\\phi^2$\nb) if $\\phi \\in (c, + \\infty)$, c is quite large positive number, then $r(\\phi)=k\\phi+o(\\phi)$, as $\\phi \\to \\infty$.</p>\n\n<p>I am looking for approximation between a &amp; b. How the Archimedes' Spiral transforms into other curve. \nAny ideas are highly welcomed.</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>If you go to the Archimedean Spiral page of the Virtual Math Museum:</p>\n\n<p><a href=\"http://virtualmathmuseum.org/Curves/archimedean_spiral/archimedean_spiral.html\" rel=\"nofollow\">http://virtualmathmuseum.org/Curves/archimedean_spiral/archimedean_spiral.html</a></p>\n\n<p>you will find a lot of material about Archimede's Spiral and the more general class of Archimedean spirals to which it belongs, and these may be of use to you. In particular, try experimenting with the 3D-XplorMath-j applet that is there, and download the file Archimedean_Spiral.pdf.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://mathoverflow.net/questions/33129/intuitive-proof-that-the-first-n-2-coordinates-on-a-sphere-are-uniform-in-a-ba","title":"Intuitive proof that the first (n-2) coordinates on a sphere are uniform in a ball","creationDate":1279913350,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"mathoverflow","questionID":33129,"body":"<p>It is a classical fact that if $(x_1,\\ldots,x_n)$ is a random vector uniformly distributed on the sphere $S^{n-1} \\subseteq \\mathbb{R}^n$, then the random vector $(x_1,\\ldots,x_{n-2})$ is uniformly distributed in the unit ball $B_{n-2} = \\{ (y_1,\\ldots,y_{n-2}) \\mid \\sum_{i=1}^{n-2} y_i^2 \\le 1\\} \\subseteq \\mathbb{R}^{n-2}$. In measure-theoretic language, the pull-back of volume measure on $B_{n-2}$ via the coordinate projection $S^{n-1} \\to B_{n-2}$, $(x_1,\\ldots,x_n) \\mapsto (x_1,\\ldots,x_{n-2})$ is Hausdorff measure on $S^{n-1}$ (up to normalization). Apparently the $n=3$ case was known to Archimedes.</p>\n\n<p>Is there an intuitive geometric proof of this, that in particular explains why you drop 2 coordinates, as opposed to 1 or 3 or ...?  Or even some heuristic that explains the 2?</p>\n\n<p>I already know reasonably slick <em>probabilistic</em> proofs of this result, including a version for $\\ell_p$ norms when $p$ is an integer and you project onto the first $n-p$ coordinates (using the right distribution on the $\\ell_p$ sphere, which is not surface area except for $p=1,2$), but as far as I can see they just make it look like a coincidence that things turn out this way.  (And as far as I know, maybe it is.)</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>One viewpoint, which is a bit gauche for your construction but valid, is that the general result is a corollary of Archimedes' theorem, that the projection from a 2-sphere $S^2$ to an interval $I$ is measure-preserving.  Whether or not you view it as a coincidence, Archimedes' theorem has an important generalization.  Namely, $S^2$ is the simplest example of a projective toric variety, and the coordinate projection is its toric moment map.  The moment map of any projective toric variety is measure preserving.  For instance, the moment map from $\\mathbb{C}P^n$ to the $n$-simplex shows you that the Fubini-Study volume of the former is $\\pi^n/n!$.  You might also recognize this as the volume of the unit ball $B_{2n}$.  There is a simple symplectic map from $B_{2n}$ to $\\mathbb{C}P^n$ which is 1-to-1 in the interior and quotients the boundary to $\\mathbb{C}P^{n-1}$.  (I learned/realized these facts in <a href=\"http://www.lehigh.edu/~dmd1/yk312.txt\">an old discussion with Doug Ravenel and Yael Karshon</a>.)</p>\n\n<p>So you could say that the original relation has a good explanation in complex and symplectic geometry, and that the explanation has been disguised a bit in real geometry.  Moreover, that 2 arises because $\\dim_\\mathbb{R} \\mathbb{C} = 2$.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://mathoverflow.net/questions/28758/uppercase-point-labels-in-high-school-diagrams-from-euclid","title":"Uppercase Point Labels in High-School Diagrams: from Euclid?","creationDate":1276964146,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"mathoverflow","questionID":28758,"body":"<p>I wonder if the convention of labeling points in geometric\ndiagrams with uppercase symbols ultimately derives from \nGreek mathematics, which was originally written in\n\"majuscule\" (uppercase) Greek script (in contrast to the \"minuscule\"\nscript that was introduced much later (9th century?)).\nCertainly Euclid and Archimedes used only uppercase,\nand all of Descartes diagrams in <em>La Geometrie</em> (1637)\nfollow the same convention.</p>\n\n<p>It seems that middle- and high-school textbooks continue\nto use uppercase labels (is this only in the U.S.?), \nbut college texts do not follow\nthis as rigidly.  This was brought home to me when I wrote\na chapter for high-school teachers and the editors changed\nall my lowercase vertex labels to uppercase.\nI much prefer lowercase for point labels, although\nI do not quite know why I have this preference.\n(Maybe because uppercase seems like SHOUTING?)\nBut when writing for an audience accustomed to\na particular convention, it seems prudent to follow that convention.</p>\n\n<p>My questions are: \n(1) Is the Greek majuscule script the origin of the uppercase\ndiagram-labeling convention?\n(2) In so far as I am correct that the uppercase convention\nis followed up to high school but dissolves at more advanced\nlevels, why does it persist to one level but dissolve beyond?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>The convention of labeling points in geometric diagrams with uppercase symbols derives (at least also) from Greek mathematics. I cannot judge about the question whether this is the \"ultimate\" reason. But if we look into Euclid's elements, we find many diagrams where all points and also all lengths (magnitudes) are labeled with Greek uppercase symbols. It is said that Euler introduced the convention to label points of triangles with uppecase Latin letters and sides with lower case Latin letters and angles with lower case Greek letters. Probably the middle- and high-school textbooks, as many other elements of mathematics, adhere to the custom invented by Euler. But when we have a closer look into his books, first of all his INTRODUCTIO IN ANALYSIN INFINITORUM, we find that he used also lower case symbols to label points. So we should drop the word \"ultimately\" from the original question.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://mathoverflow.net/questions/16891/sum-of-odd-number-is-a-square-whos-theorem-is-this","title":"Sum of odd number is a square, whos theorem is this?","creationDate":1267554966,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"mathoverflow","questionID":16891,"body":"<p>1=1^2\n1+3=2^2\n1+3+5=3^2\n1+3+5+7=4^2</p>\n\n<p>Who's is this theorem, who is the first to realyse this? Nicomah, Aristotel, Archimedes? I need to know for sure and fast plz. thanx</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>The original source, attributing this theorem to the early pythagoreans is Theon of Smyrna. \nEduard Hiller: Expositio Rerum Mathematicarum ad legendum Platonem utilium. Rec. Theon Smyrnaeus, reprinted by Teubner, Stuttgart, 1995.</p>\n\n<p>Further Aristoteles writes, with reference to the early pythagoreans too: \"the gnomons are placed round the one\" explaining in a somewhat dark manner the geometric aspect of the sum of odd numbers, placed around the 1 <a href=\"http://pinkmonkey.com/dl/library1/aris20.pdf\" rel=\"nofollow\">Physics, book 3, chapter 4</a> as sketched in the answer by Stefan .</p>\n\n<p>So there is no chance to find an individual name of the first inventor other than Pythagoras himself. If it's a granular or liquid material and the screw is tilted to a side, it will just roll/slide/flow along the blade, to remain on the bottom side of the tube. </p>\n\n<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/KE01J.gif\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></p>\n\n<p>But then, <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedes%27_screw#Variants\">Wikipedia</a> says:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>A variant of the Archimedes' screw can also be found in some injection molding machines, die casting machines and extrusion of plastics, which employ a screw of decreasing pitch to compress and melt the material. Finally, it is also used in a specific type of positive displacement air compressor: the rotary-screw air compressor. On a much larger scale, Archimedes' screws of decreasing pitch are used for the compaction of waste material.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>In such case the forces - pressure, shearing, viscosity, adhesion, all would outweigh gravity, and probably in some cases friction against the tube walls. For example, in injection molding, what is there to keep the half-molten plastic from forming a clumping mass, sticking to the screw and keeping rotating in place without any progress? When the pressure increases, which force prevents the material from backing up into area of lower pressure?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>The ones I've seen that actually cause significant pressure don't have a smooth cylinder wall.  The walls have grooves at right angles to the blade passing over them.  It's a tradeoff since the grooves also provide a leakage path around the blade.  But with a combination of a smooth and minimum-friction blade plus a grooved wall, the thing works.  Ideally the walls have maximum friction parallel to the blade and none perpendicular to it.</p>\n\n<p>Apparently we can achieve this well enough that such things actually work.  I remember wondering the same thing about my mother's old hand-cranked meat grinder, probably made in Germany in the early 1900s.  The blade and walls were made of the same metal, and the blade wasn't particularly polished or anything.  The walls did have grooves, which was enough to make the whole thing work.  It produced enough pressure to force the meat thru small openings at the end.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://engineering.stackexchange.com/questions/470/could-a-gold-second-metal-crown-be-created-that-would-pass-the-archimedes-test","title":"Could a gold/{second metal} crown be created that would pass the Archimedes test cost effectively?","creationDate":1423133463,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"engineering","questionID":470,"body":"<p>Many people are familiar with the story of <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedes#Discoveries_and_inventions\" rel=\"nofollow\">Archimedes submerging a crown in water</a> to identify if the volume of the crown was consistent with the volume of the same amount of gold. If mathematics arises from our notion of space, that is prior to language, and to culture.  If our notion of accumulating area is inborn, and becomes mathematics when we notice it and express it in language, then whether mathematics controls our thinking depends on which side of the fence of language you are talking about.</p>\n\n<p>From that point of view, we have a shared intuition of area, and we have, separately, formalized ways that try to keep that intuition free and unaffected by individual experience so that it can be shared.  The former clearly controls thought, the latter helps this happen more easily, but probably contributes little control of its own.</p>\n\n<p>At the same time, there are other aspects of language that I feel strongly shape our thinking.  I just gave an example here <a href=\"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/a/23625/9166\">http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/a/23625/9166</a>, and plenty more examples fill Wittgenstein and other language-focussed philosophers.</p>\n\n<p>There is experimental evidence that even these are a selection from among a basic range of intuitions that are promoted or suppressed selectively by exposure.  This is part of the Chomsky theory of generative-transformational grammars.  There is a trans-linguistic \"deep structure\" representation.</p>\n\n<p>So to some degree, the elements of grammar that I feel deeply shape our thinking are still in the same area as mathematics.  But we choose which aspects of deep structure are most accessible, and which ones will be used together differently in childhood by exposing ourselves to different languages which veil the underlying meaning in transformations and representations.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/21345/human-belief-systems-and-reason","title":"Human belief systems and reason","creationDate":1421786328,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":21345,"body":"<p>Belief puts people into action, but often action is needed before <em>belief</em> to develop something in order to be able to believe in it in the first place, or at least so it seems. </p>\n\n<p>It seems to me that many people like Gallileo and Archimedes didn't knew what they might find in their work, the question is how did they acted without belief or how did they develop the belief to be able to act despite so much uncertainty of what they might find being useful at all? </p>\n\n<p>Often reason isn't able to change one's belief systems. It is the belief in the reason itself that might change one's beliefs. How to develop such belief in one's reason to be able to change your own beliefs just by logically reasoning without any interference of third parties -- just you yourself and your own pure thought alone?</p>\n\n<p>What are your thoughts on this subject and are there any books, research papers, on this topic worth your recommendation? </p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>The famous mathematician and philosopher Blaise Pascal had a theory that belief follows action --if you act as a believer, you gain faith (<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal%27s_Wager\" rel=\"nofollow\">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal%27s_Wager</a> --note the section marked \"Inability to Believe\").  It seems likely that he found this to be true in his own life --certainly theologically, and perhaps as a scientist as well.</p>\n\n<p>As far as (other) scientists and innovators who have believed in their theories before the evidence arrived to confirm them --it seems fairly obvious that belief in unproven theories is common.  Those whose theories are later confirmed are celebrated as visionaries, those whose theories don't pan out are dismissed as cranks.  If there wasn't a certain base willingness among people to occasionally step out on faith, the ability of humanity to advance would be severely curtailed.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/21270/how-historically-grounded-is-the-standard-narrative-of-the-irrationals-in-antiqu","title":"How historically grounded is the standard narrative of the Irrationals in Antiquity?","creationDate":1421490685,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":21270,"body":"<p>Its commonly said that the Pythagoreans were unbalanced by the discovery of the irrationals; since their philosophy was predicated on ratios; ratios of two finite numbers.</p>\n\n<p>Still, it is natural to consider a ratio of two infinite numbers; and most of these will approach an irrational. After all, one easily consider 1,2,3,...; so one might be lead to 1/2, 11/22, 111/222 ...; and one then need only show that some infinite ratios cannot be reduced to finite ones by common techniques: for example (5 <em>x</em> 1111...)/(6 <em>x</em> 1111...) = 5/6. Now, this of course is using imprecise techniques, as far as modern contemporary mathematics is concerned; but different standards of rigor held in antiquity...</p>\n\n<p>Archimedes, much later than the Pythagoreans, had developed a method of exhaustion; a precursor to the calculus.</p>\n\n<p>One might argue this is an outcome of the 'irrational' discovery; but given the <em>apeiron</em> of Anaximander, the <em>boundless</em>; the idea of the infinite as something unbounded was already there.</p>\n\n<p>How historically grounded is the 'standard' narrative of the irrationals and the Pythagoreans? That is their entire philosophy was <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippasus\" rel=\"nofollow\">disrupted</a>:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Pythagoreans preached that all numbers could be expressed as the ratio of integers, and the discovery of irrational numbers is said to have shocked them</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>and</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>Pappus merely says that the knowledge of irrational numbers originated in the Pythagorean school, and that the member who first divulged the secret perished by drowning</p>\n</blockquote>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>According to my understanding, it is noy \"historically correct\" to say that Pythagoreans discovered the <em>irrational</em> numbers.</p>\n\n<p>Archaic Greek mathematics shared the (implicit) assumption that, given two magnitudes, e.g. two segments of lenght <strong>a</strong> and <strong>b</strong> respectively, it is always possible to find a segment of \"unit lenght\" <strong>u</strong> such that \"it measures\" both, i.e. such that [using modern algebraic formulae which are totally foreign to Greek math] :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p><strong>a=n×u</strong> and <strong>b=m×u</strong>, for suitable <strong>n,m</strong>.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>From the above assumption, it follows that :</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p><strong>a/b = n×u / m×u = n/m</strong>.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>The assumption amounts to saying that the ratio between two magnitudes is always a ratio between <em>integers</em> (i.e. in modern terms: a <em>rational</em> number).</p>\n\n<p>But note that for Greek math the <strong>only</strong> numbers are the <em>natural</em> ones and they must be distinguished from <em>magnitudes</em> : a segment, a square, ... which are \"measured by\" numbers expressing the ratio between the measured magnitude and the relevant \"unit\" magnitude.</p>\n\n<p>For ancient Greeks there are <strong>no</strong> rational numbers; but only magnitudes measurable with multiples of a suitable unit one.</p>\n\n<p>The discovery of the existence of irrational magnitudes, through the proof that the case where <strong>b</strong> is the side of the square and <strong>a</strong> its diagonal is not expressible as a ratio between (natural) numbers, leads Greek mathematics to the withdrawal of the above (implicit) assumption, that we may call : \"commensurability assumption\" and to the axiomatization of geometry, i.e. the systematic effort to explicitly lists all the needed assumptions.</p>\n\n<p>In conclusion, in ancient Greek mathematics there were no \"irrational\" numbers, nor \"inifinte\" ones.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/7547/is-there-any-evidence-that-copernicus-knew-of-the-heliocentric-hypothesis-elabor","title":"Is there any evidence that Copernicus knew of the Heliocentric hypothesis elaborated in Antiquity?","creationDate":1373715760,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":7547,"body":"<p>Copernicus is commonly favoured with the discovery of the heliocentric theory. However, it's well known that the hypothesis was already discussed in Greek Antiquity, primarily by <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos\" rel=\"nofollow\">Aristarchus</a> (300 BC) and supported by a direct quotation in Archimedes' <em>Sand-Reckoner</em> (which implies a certain level of substantial support for that hypothesis at that time, considering Archimedes' stature), and, according to Wikipedia, by Islamic and Indian astronomists: <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aryabhata\" rel=\"nofollow\">Aryabhata</a> (500 AD) in India and by Ibn al-Shatir in the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maragheh_observatory\" rel=\"nofollow\">Maragheh</a> school in Persia. Although, here the evidence is contentious, and leans towards a more efficient interpretation of Ptolemy's geocentric model rather than an outright acceptance of a heliocentric one. </p>\n\n<p>Given the prominence of Archimedes as a Hellenic mathematician, and the proximity and efflorescence of Islam before the Italian Renaissance, is there any evidence that links Copernicus with these predecessors? [Source: <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism\" rel=\"nofollow\">Wikipedia</a>]</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>From Wolfram|Alpha:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>We know for a fact that Copernicus was well aware of Aristarchus's priority, since his original draft of De Revolutionibus has survived and features a passage referring to Aristarchus which Copernicus crossed out so as not to compromise the originality of his theory. [Source: <a href=\"http://scienceworld.wolfram.com/biography/Copernicus.html\" rel=\"nofollow\">Wolfram|Alpha</a>]</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<hr>\n\n<p><em>Edit by Gugg:</em> I think I might have found the evidence. It may very well be this fragment (lines 11-13). I (well, ManishEarth) pulled it off the Internet, but it wasn't accompanied by <em>any</em> commentary, so I'm not even 100% sure this is by Copernicus. However, I'm quite sure about lines 12 and 13 containing Philolaus, mobility, Earth (or ground?), and Aristarchus. Feel free to comment on whether this indeed is the evidence. If somebody can read and/or translate it, that would be great.</p>\n\n<p><img src=\"http://i.stack.imgur.com/49uED.gif\" alt=\"enter image description here\"></p>\n"},{"link":"http://philosophy.stackexchange.com/questions/6546/if-there-is-progress-in-philosophy-why-is-there-no-philosophers-or-thinkers-as","title":"If there is progress in philosophy, Why is there no philosophers or thinkers as important today as in the past?","creationDate":1365943831,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"philosophy","questionID":6546,"body":"<p>Why don't we have thinkers in the present time (or recent times) that have the same caliber as Albert Einstein, Archimedes, Socrates, Shakespeare, Freud, Aristotle, Plato, Darwin, Popper and all the other great minds over the past few thousand years?</p>\n","isAnswered":false},{"link":"http://worldbuilding.stackexchange.com/questions/22323/how-does-the-medieval-militants-use-mirror-as-weapon","title":"How does the medieval militants use mirror as weapon?","creationDate":1439298520,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"worldbuilding","questionID":22323,"body":"<p>Set between 4th to 6th century silvered glass mirror has been invented and were seen in battles all over Europe. How do the medieval militants use the mirror to turn the tides of war? (Archimedes's heat ray?)</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>If you put mirrors on your soldier's shields, and face them towards the sun, they can blind their enemies with the reflected sunlight (for a great example of the effectiveness of this tactic, see the battle of Helm's Deep in the film adaptation of Lord of the Rings:The Two Towers). There's not much that can be done to combat this tactic, as sunglasses won't be invented for another few centuries, and you sort of have to be looking at your enemy to fight them. </p>\n\n<p>The only flaw to this strategy, and I think it's one Sun Tzu warns about, is that you should never point your army at the sun. Worst case scenario, you'll fight a battle where no one is able to see. But if you get the angle right, the sun should be high enough to not get in your eyes, but reflect into the enemy's.</p>\n\n<p>I can only think of a few reasons why this wasn't actually done in the past: 1)- mirrors were too expensive, and 2)- soldiers were too dirty. 2 can be solved by holding some water in reserve, and washing the shields right before battle, but 1 is really up to you; get as much mirror as you think the army can afford, and divide it up among your front line.  </p>\n"},{"link":"http://worldbuilding.stackexchange.com/questions/15261/would-a-medieval-society-based-on-simple-hydroponic-be-posible","title":"Would a medieval society based on simple hydroponic be posible","creationDate":1430138490,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"worldbuilding","questionID":15261,"body":"<p>In relation to a previous question: <a href=\"http://worldbuilding.stackexchange.com/questions/9573/would-a-medieval-arcology-be-possible\">Would a medieval Arcology be possible?</a> we established that without a water source it would not be posible to have a society.</p>\n\n<p>We also established that we need a certain number of square miles per person to be able to sustain food production: <a href=\"https://worldbuilding.stackexchange.com/questions/9582/how-many-people-can-you-feed-per-square-kilometer-of-farmland\">How many people can you feed per square-kilometer of farmland?</a></p>\n\n<p>Pack-animals used for travel between cities can be used for food source when an animal is no longer able to make the travel. This amount of meat can be a significantpart of the food intake.</p>\n\n<p><strong>The question:</strong>\nWould it be possible to have sufficient food production in a city, locked inside walls, given that every possible surface such as roofs, eg. tomato pots in windows, and basically every free surface was used for some kind of food production.</p>\n\n<p>Archimedes screws or similar that are hand powered (maybe from child labor), to supply water to the roofs, and people are carrying buckets with water around the city, constantly watering.</p>\n\n<p>Assume the cities inside the walls have houses so close, there is no room for gardens. </p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>The answers to \"How many people can you feed per square-kilometer of farmland?\" come to different conclusions, but they all are in the range of several hundred to 1000 m² to feed one person. Modern (!) hydroponics might reduce that to a 3rd. So let's be really, really optimistic and assume 100m².</p>\n\n<p>How much roof area you have per person can easily be measured by how much living-space you have per person.</p>\n\n<p>My apartment is 60 m². I live in a multi-story apartment building, but buildings with more than 2 floors were quite rare in the middle-age. So the roof area available for me would also be about 60 m².</p>\n\n<p>60 m² per person would be quite decadent for the middle ages. It is even quite decadent for todays standards, because I am living alone. Even today a four-person family living in the city rarely has an apartment with more than 100 m². I would rather estimate the floor-area and by induction also the roof-area per person in a medieval town to be a lot less, more in the area of 20 m².</p>\n\n<p>Sure, we excluded any public buildings, but we also did not take residential buildings with more than one floor into account, so these two factors should cancel out.</p>\n\n<p>20 m² is simply not enough to feed the person living below it, even with high-tech methods, let alone medieval methods. The only way to increase the output would be with multi-story farming and artificial light sources, but the technology to create artificial UV light didn't exist back then.</p>\n"},{"link":"http://worldbuilding.stackexchange.com/questions/11916/what-would-it-take-to-actually-move-the-earth-using-a-lever","title":"What would it take to actually move the Earth using a lever?","creationDate":1426342317,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"worldbuilding","questionID":11916,"body":"<p>Archimedes claimed that he would lift the Earth if he could find a suitable place outside it, with his knowledge of mechanics.</p>\n\n<p>So, my question is: What kind of set-up (e.g. how large lever or how massive support to stand etc.) would Archimedes require to carry out this project? And how long he would have to keep pursuing (assume that he is alone doing all these) to move the Earth by a fair amount? </p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>Ted-Ed actually did a video on levers. You can see it (fast-forwarded to the answer of this very question) <a href=\"https://youtu.be/YlYEi0PgG1g?t=3m31s\">here</a>!</p>\n\n<p>Their length of a lever around <em>quadrillion light-years</em>, for a lever which uses the moon as a fulcrum, is really infeasible. By infeasible, I mean it has multiple problems. The first is simply getting that much material without it bending, twisting, or snapping. The second is getting a material that has the strength needed to act as a fulcrum. The third is getting enough gravity on the Archimedes end of things so he can exert that force on that lever.</p>\n\n<p>As far as I can tell, there is no material that would be that strong. So, while lifting the earth with a large enough lever is possible, making that large lever is not. Sorry, Archie!</p>\n"},{"link":"http://hermeneutics.stackexchange.com/questions/1514/is-the-author-of-ecclesiastes-referring-to-archimedes","title":"Is the author of Ecclesiastes referring to Archimedes?","creationDate":1334125656,"checked":false,"isBad":false,"isGood":false,"site":"hermeneutics","questionID":1514,"body":"<p>The following passage is <a href=\"http://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_%28Wikisource%29/Ecclesiastes#Chapter_9\" rel=\"nofollow\">Ecclesiastes 9:14</a></p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>A small city, and few people in it, and a great king comes there, and encircled it and built upon it great fortifications. And he found in it, a poor wise man, who delivered the city in his wisdom, and nobody remembered this poor man.</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>Upon reading this, I immediately thought of Archimedes. I found people calling this <a href=\"http://www.godrules.net/library/clarke/clarkeecc9.htm\" rel=\"nofollow\">an analogy</a>.</p>\n\n<p>But considering the late date of Ecc, could this be a direct reference to the siege of Syracuse? Is it consistent with the dating of the text to identify the \"poor wise man\" as Archimedes?</p>\n","isAnswered":true,"answer":"<p>According to <em>The Interpreter's Bible</em>, the language is written in a late form of Hebrew and so:</p>\n\n<blockquote>\n  <p>As judged by it's language, the book of Ecclesiastes is much later than the work of the Chronicler and later than the book of Esther (<em>ca.</em> 300 B.C.).</p>\n</blockquote>\n\n<p>If you agree with the 3rd century B.C. dating of Ecclesiastes, then perhaps it could be seen as an analogy, however I would argue against it being direct reference because of two points:</p>\n\n<ol>\n<li>Archimedes is famous today and was famous then - orders were given for him not to be harmed (although despite this he was killed)</li>\n<li>In Ecclesiastes 9:15 the little city was delivered. This was not the case for Syracuse.</li>\n</ol>\n"}]
